RUSSIAN FEDERATION
GAS DISTRIBUTION REHABILITATION AND
ENERGY EFFICIENCY PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:         The Russian Federation
Beneficiaries:    Volgograd Gorgas, Local District Heating and Power Utilities.
Amount:           US$106.5 million
Terms:            Currency Pool Loan for 17 years, including 5 years grace, at the Bank's standard
variable interest rate.
Onlending:        The proceeds of the loan directed toward gas distribution rehabilitation would be
onlent to Volgograd Gorgas with an amortization period of 17 years, including 5
years grace. The energy efficiency component would be onlent to municipal district
heating and power utilities with an amortization of 10 years, including 3 years
grace. Funds onlent would carry an interest rate equal to the Bank lending rate plus
a premium of 250 basis points.
Objectives:       The principal objectives of the project are to increase the efficiency of energy use
in Russia, to improve the efficiency and safety of gas distribution, and to support
policy reforms in the gas subsector. The project is expected to have both direct
impact in the targeted cities and indirect impact elsewhere in Russia through the
demonstration of technological and managerial principles.
Description:      The project consists of three components: (a) gas distribution  network
improvements; (b) energy efficiency investments; and (c) technical assistance. The
gas distribution network investments largely focus on metering, cathodic protection,
and pipe replacement. Energy efficiency investments would focus on replacement
of outdated and inefficient equipment. Technical assistance is designed both to
support project implementation and to advance the government's reform program.
An initial program aimed at greenhouse gas reduction, and financed under the
Global Environment Facility, is also linked to the project.
Benefits:         Direct project benefits arising through metering and through support of energy
efficiency investments include opportunities to: (a) increase availability of low-cost
gas for export; (b) defer investment in new gas supply; and/or, (c) displace
petroleum products or coal in domestic markets, with resultant benefits in terms of
environmental impacts and energy export opportunities. The investments in gas
distribution rehabilitation would yield benefits in terms of increased economic life
for network assets, as well as reduced investment costs through improved control
and planning. The acquisition of leak detection equipment would also help to
improve safety and reduce leakage of methane into the atmosphere.
Risks:            While it is expected that all project components would generate strong economic
returns and acceptable financial returns, risks are not inconsiderable. Risks faced



- v -
by the project include those associated with equipment prices, gas price reform,
macroeconomic uncertainties, changes to taxes, and efficiency of implementation.
The risks are being addressed explicitly through up-front actions and conditions, a
mid-term implementation review, consulting assistance, and a twinning arrangement
with a foreign gas distribution company.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
GAS DISTRIBUTION REHABILITATION AND ENERGY EFFICIENCY PROJECT
I. THE MACROECONOMIC SETTING
1.1   Following the collapse of the former Soviet Union (FSU) in late 1991, the Russian government
launched a radical program of economic reform to replace the remnants of the command economy with
a market-oriented economy based on the private ownership of property. From the beginning, this effort
took place in decidedly unfavorable conditions and, while results have been mixed, there have been
significant achievements in a number of specific sectors.
1.2    A great deal was accomplished during the period 1992 - 1993 to move Russia toward a market
economy. Prices have been liberalized, and there have been major shifts in relative prices, especially for
the critical energy and service sectors. Privatization has led to a rapid transfer of state assets into private
hands. Foreign trade has been substantially liberalized. Finally, enterprises are making decisions
increasingly on the basis of commercial criteria. Macroeconomic problems have persisted, but can be
largely traced to structural distortions that were inherited from the past. These distortions are being
addressed and progress is being made in rectifying them, but full adjustment must be regarded as a long-
term process. In the near term, various political factions are likely to express, and occasionally impose,
alternative paths for development. However, it is expected that Russia will continue to move toward a
market economy, both as a result of existing momentum, and for lack of a feasible alternative.
1.3    Within this evolving pattern of development, key sectors will have a direct positive impact at the
macroeconomic and microeconomic level. Energy is one such sector, and within energy, the gas
subsector is critical.  Gas is the primary source of fuel in the domestic market, and in 1993 accounted
for approximately 20 percent of foreign exchange earnings. The proposed Gas Distribution Rehabilitation
and Energy Efficiency Project, the subject of this report, is consistent with the goals of sector and
macroeconomic reform and recovery. The project would demonstrate the potential for short-term supply
enhancements by improving the efficiency of gas utilization, while supporting Russia's sector-reform
programs in the areas of price, structure, commercialization at the enterprise level, and reduced adverse
environmental impact.
II. THE ENERGY SECTOR
2.1    Energy is estimated to account for 15 percent of Russia's GDP, 11 percent of industrial
production, and 50 percent of its convertible foreign exchange earnings. Rapid stabilization of the overall
economy is unlikely to occur unless macroeconomic reforms are paralleled by energy- sector reform and
recovery.
A.    Energy Resources, Production and Exports
2.2   It is estimated that Russia has 10 percent of the world's oil reserves, 33 percent of natural gas
reserves, 10 percent of hard coal reserves, and 20 percent of the world's brown coal reserves. Its
capacity to generate electric power ranks second in the world. Russia is the second largest energy
producer in the world, accounting for about 14 percent of total commercial energy production. Despite



- 2 -
having one of the world's most energy intensive economies, Russia has been able to export over 25
percent of its total production, making it the largest exporter of energy. Energy exports, primarily of
oil and gas, represent almost 50 percent of Russia's current exports to non-Commonwealth of Independent
States (CIS) countries, thus providing critical foreign exchange.
2.3    Maintaining productive capacity and developing unexploited energy reserves will require massive
investments. Annual capital requirements for oil and gas alone, merely to stabilize existing production
levels, are probably in the excess of US$10 billion.  Funds are no longer available to meet these
requirements, however; and the future prospects for the sector are grim. Output of energy is now falling
in all energy subsectors. Crude oil output is down more than 45 percent from its 1988 peak and has been
declining at an annual rate of about 15 percent for the past three years. This decline must be viewed as
catastrophic if sustained for another year or two. Natural gas production, which had steadily increased
over the past several years, declined by over 3 percent 1993, and is estimated to have fallen by a similar
percentage in 1994. Production of coal has also been declining by approximately 10 percent each year,
indicating a deepening crisis in this subsector as well. Finally, Russia's electricity-generating capacity
has only been adequate owing to a drop in consumption of about 6 percent per year. Without this
contraction in demand, the poorly maintained power plants and ageing asset base would be inadequate.
Data on Russia's energy production and net exports for the years 1990 to 1993 are contained in Annex
2-1.
2.4    Energy exports in 1993 consisted of: natural gas, estimated at 51 percent; oil and petroleum
products, 42 percent; coal, 5 percent; and electricity, 2 percent (based on tons of oil equivalence).
Natural gas is expected to become increasingly important, in both export and domestic markets, because
of its environmental attractiveness. As a result, the strategic value of gas to the Russian economy
requires special attention.
B.    Energy Consumption
2.5    Data on consumption of commercial energy in Russia for 1990 through 1993 are presented, by
type, in Annex 2-1. Energy consumption decreased by 12 percent between 1990 and 1993, from 1,121
million tons of oil equivalent (mtoe) to 984 mtoe. The largest declines were in the coal sector (23
percent) and petroleum products (32 percent). Gas consumption remained relatively constant during this
period (as interfuel substitution has taken place in the interim). In 1993, gas accounted for about 55
percent of domestic consumption', petroleum about 18 percent and coal 17 percent. The remaining 10
percent was largely primary electricity supply (nuclear and hydroelectric power). While gas volumes
were down in 1993 relative to 1990, the share was 6 percentage points higher. The prospects for
continued increases in market share for natural gas are good because of its relative price advantages and
favorable environmental impacts.
2.6   Inefficient energy use is prevalent throughout the Russian economy. In 1992, Russia's energy
use per unit of GDP was 14 times that of Japan, 8 times that of the United Kingdom, and 5 times that
of the United States. This problem has since worsened, with considerable decreases in economic output
and only modest decreases in energy consumption. Russia's inefficient energy use stems from a
combination of factors, including an emphasis on energy-intensive industries, widespread use of inefficient
and outdated equipment, prices that discourage efficient use of energy, and an absence of metering.
I /   Includes gas used in thermal power generation and in petrochemical production.



- 3 -
2.7    The potential for the economic rationalization of energy consumption in Russia is substantial.
The government has initiated a program of reforms to address this prospect encompassing: (a) energy
price adjustments, through liberalization of pricing mechanisms, reduction in subsidies and reduction in
export constraints; (b) preparation of draft legislation on energy conservation; and, (c) establishment of
the Russian Energy Savings Foundation (RESF) to encourage energy efficiency investments.
C.    Energy Prices, Taxes, and Subsidies
2.8    Significant adjustments have been made to relative energy prices over the past three to four years.
In 1991 energy prices were less than 10 percent of estimated market levels. By early 1995, domestic gas
prices to industrial and commercial customers had reached approximately two-thirds of estimated full cost
recovery, and domestic prices for petroleum products were at similar levels. Electricity prices varied
among regions, but were typically around 60 percent of full cost recovery on sales to industry. Energy
prices to households, however, have risen at a more gradual rate. The government has justified the
slower increase in household prices in light of the slow adjustment in labor prices, the absence of an
effective safety net and the resultant concerns over household ability-to-pay for energy. Given that the
poor typically spend a disproportionate share of their income on energy, this approach is acceptable until
an adequate safety net is established.
2.9    Taxation of oil production has been prohibitive. This policy has resulted in financial losses by
producing enterprises, and sharply curtailed investment. Petroleum tax rates are very high, and, since
they are based on revenues rather than profits, are insensitive to the variations in production and
operating costs across the sector. Financial support has been extended to the sectcr through direct budget
transfers, as is the case for coal, or through subsidized credits, which have been granted to the oil and
gas subsectors. Overall, there is a pressing need to continue to rationalize energy prices and reform
taxes; however, these policies entail far-reaching macroeconomic and structural implications. Recent
actions on tax reforms and reductions in subsidized credit have improved the situation considerably.
D.    Energy and the Environment
2.10   Russia's current environmental problems are serious and widespread, and result from past policies
that set performance targets in quantitative terms and did not place an economic value on the
environment, lack of environmental legislation, standards and management systems, and pricing policies
that undervalued natural resources. Russia's energy inefficiency, in both production and end-use, not
only accelerates the depletion of economic resources, but also represents a prime cause of pollution and
of natural resource degradation. Price reform, coupled with investment in more efficient technologies,
would help to correct this problem, and the government is therefore actively pursuing initiative in both
of these areas. Furthermore, environmental legislation is being steadily improved, both in terms of
establishing standards and enforcing their implementation.
E.    Organization of the Energy Sector and Structural Reform
2.11   Prior to 1990, energy was fully integrated into the FSU's national plan. The sector was managed
by central government ministries and by subsector or branch ministries. This centralization of authority,
together with the lack of coordination among those central ministries with authority over energy
subsectors, stifled commercial and operational initiatives in the sector and led to the pressure to
decentralize.



- 4 -
2.12   Russia's energy sector is undergoing structural reform to ameliorate these problems. Goals of
the reform package include reduced state intervention, the promotion of competition, and a strong,
independent private sector. Since 1991, progress has been made regarding each of these objectives.
While policy matters remain under the jurisdiction of the ministries, branch ministries were converted
to large state-owned enterprises, which were to address commercial and operational matters for each
subsector. The new enterprises included: Rosneftegas, with jurisdiction over crude oil production;
Glavtransneft, crude oil transport; Rosnefteproduct, oil product transport and distribution; Gazprom, non-
associated gas production, transmission, storage and export; Rosstrojgasifikazia, gas distribution2; Ugol
Rossii, coal; and Rossenergo, electric power. Although distanced from the ministries, these state or
national concerns were wholly owned by the state and were still formally accountable to them. Selected
activities came under newly established committees rather than state concerns; for example, oil refining
under the Refining Committee within the Ministry of Fuel and Power, and oil exploration under the State
Committee on Geology (the former Ministry of Geology).
2.13   Following the creation of the state concerns, a period of further decentralization of control over
the sector began, which was fostered by certain parts of the reform movement in Government that
promoted competition. Other factions in the government, however, regarded this development as
potentially disruptive during a time when stability in energy supplies was vital, and thus argued for a
slowdown to the decentralization process. By and large, the Government has acceded to the latter
proposal.
2.14   As of the present, all of the state energy enterprises have been converted into joint stock
companies (JSC's). This is beneficial in that it sets the stage for corporatization of the enterprises along
coommercial lines and for their ultimate privatization. At the same time, a controlling interest in each of
the new JSCs is to be retained by the state. For the most part, administrative responsibility for the state's
shareholdings has been placed in the hands of a few centralized holding companies, which are themselves
either wholly owned or effectively controlled by the state. Privatization has entered a new phase in which
the government intends to sell its remaining equity stake in profitable enterprises at a price that would
provide a reasonable return on its original investment. This project is designed to further this objective
(see para 6.8).
F.     Foreign Direct Investment
2.15   Foreign direct investment in Russia's energy sector can make a critical difference: in funding;
in transferring technology; in promoting a competitive environment; and in transferring managerial skills
appropriate to a market economy. To date, however, the energy sector, like the rest of the economy,
has had little success in attracting major foreign investment. Investors have attributed this to the absence
of an accommodating legal and fiscal framework, to institutional uncertainties, and to understandable
confusions during a period of major transition. An important factor often mentioned by potential foreign
investors in the energy sector is the conflicting signals they receive from Russia; in the end, the investors
are not persuaded that their investments are welcomed.
2/     With the exception of Moscow and St. Petersburg, which were the responsibility of Mosgas and
Lengas, respectively.



- 5 -
G.    Bank's Role in the Energy Sector
2.16   The Bank's energy strategy in Russia has been to support sector reform and recovery through
direct assistance to the Russian authorities in designing their reform program and in developing a focused
program of energy lending. The Bank assigned high priority to the oil and gas subsectors owing to: (a)
the potential to attract significant inflow of foreign investment capital; (b) their potential to generate
foreign exchange through exports; (c) the potential impact on the fiscal deficit through adjustment of
domestic prices and rationalization of taxes; and, (d) the prospect of an early impact on sector revenues
and export earnings.
2.17  Identification of suitable projects for lending has proceeded in parallel with technical cooperation.
The US$1 billion project to support urgently required oil production rehabilitation in western Siberia is
effective and procurement is advancing favorably. A second oil production rehabilitation project has also
been approved for which procurement is well advanced. Other projects in the oil subsector have been
identified including: (a) a joint venture for new field development; (b) a refinery restructuring investment;
and, (c) oil transport. In the gas sector, Gazprom, the entity responsible for production and transmission,
has been able to attract financing from other sources, leaving the Bank to focus on gas distribution and
energy efficiency investments.
2.18   The Bank has broadened its energy sector dialogue with the government to include other
subsectors, such as power and coal.3 Lending to the energy sector could range between US$500 million
and US$1 billion annually for the next several years. As indicated in the most recent Country Assistance
Strategy (CAS), the energy sector is expected to be a major focus of Bank lending, accounting for
roughly 40 percent of new commitments during the FY93-97 period. In particular, the CAS recognizes
that developments in the energy sector are critical to Russia's export prospects over the next several years
and therefore to the viability of the external financing program. In all cases, the highest priority would
be assigned to designing a lending program that would act as a catalyst to private sector investment.
Once the macroeconomic framework stabilizes and an acceptable investment framework has been
established, the Bank's relative role in Russia's energy sector should decline significantly, giving way to
private funding. The proposed Project is fully consistent with the Bank's policy objectives in the recent
CAS and represents a priority use of Bank resources in assisting the transformation of the Russian
economy.
111. GAS SUBSECTOR
A.    Gas Resources, Production and Exports
3.1    As of the beginning of 1993, Russia's gas reserves were estimated at 48 trillion cubic meters, 33
percent of l; e world's reserves. Gas production was 617 billion cubic meters (BCM) in 1993, down 3.5
percent from 1992, giving a reserve-to-production ratio of 78 years. The majority of production takes
place in western Siberia: in 1992, the Urenguoy field produced 288 BCM and Yamburg produced 178
BCM. Although associated gas production has typically been about 6 percent of total gas production,
it is estimated that a further 3 percent could be recovered by recapturing flared gas. In 1993, gas exports
from Russia included 65 BCM to Western Europe, and 36 BCM to Central Europe. Russian gas accounts
for 90 percent of the total with the remaining 10 percent transited through Russia from Turkmenistan.
3/     A portion of the Bank's first technical cooperation project (TCP) in Russia, approved by the Bank's
Board of Directors in September 1992, was allocated to coal.



- 6 -
Compared with 1992, 1993 exports to Europe were up 5 percent. Exports to cther FSU republics,
however, fell by approximately 25 percent (from 106 BCM to 79 BCM), mainly owing to economic
restructuring and payment problems. Initial figures for 1994 indicate a further 3 percent growth in
exports to Western Europe, and a 10 percent increase in sales to Central Europe. Exports to FSU
republics in 1994 were at approximately the same level as in 1993.
3.2    Fields now in production are expected to meet demand through most of the remainder of the
decade, as gas consumption in the FSU continues to be rationalized. The Yamburg field alone has the
potential to deliver another 60 to 80 BCM/yr, roughly equivalent to the level of Russia's current exports
to Western Europe. Step-outs from the existing fields will also keep the cost of gas supply low for the
near to medium term. Russia's large unexploited fields (Yamal and Shtokmanskaya in the Barents Sea)
are remote and present considerable environmental problems. Postponing the need to mobilize these
resources would enable Gazprom to further address the environmental problems associated with the new
fields.
B.    Natural Gas Demand
3.3    Export Markets: Although the export market during the early 1990's has been relatively weak,
gas demand outside Russia is expected to rise in the medium to long term. Forecasts of exports of
Russian gas prepared in 1992 indicated an 8 percent overall decline between 1992 and 1994, with a
gradual levelling off and beginning recovery after 1997. The decline was dominated by a sharp drop in
exports to FSU countries. (see table in Annex 3-1). Preliminary 1994 data indicate continued growth
in gas markets in Central Europe, and a slower but steady increase in Western European demand.
Markets in the FSU countries remain weak, however. Russia is a major source of natural gas for Central
and Western European markets, and is viewed as the single most important source for future gas supplies.
Alternative sources of gas, which include Algeria, Iran, the North Sea, and the Central Asian Republics,
each face transit problems. Russia, however, has the potential to supply a significant portion of the
expected growth in European gas markets, and at competitive prices, as low-cost gas becomes available
from the rationalization of existing domestic markets and the capture of flared associated gas. Assuming
sufficient transmission capacity, Russia could expect not only to maintain but also to increase its share
in the European markets. Indications are, however, that transmission capacity may be a growing
constraint. The main transit route is through the Ukraine, and there are continued allegations that the
Ukraine is appropriating transit gas for its own use, in order to make up for Russian cutbacks in
deliveries to Ukraine as a result of payment arrears. Alternative routes through Poland/Belarus are being
explored, and would require in the order of US$5 billion in investment. At present, however, the
financing and the international ownership/operating agreements needed to develop this corridor are not
yet in place.
3.4    Domestic Market: Gas has been capturing an increasing share of the primary energy market in
Russia, displacing coal in the 1970s and petroleum products in the late 1980s. With coal and oil
production decreasing, gas continues to increase market share, nearing half of Russia's total primary
consumption. This trend is expected to continue as gas-based power generation increases, both for cost
and environmental reasons. Although fuel oil is currently the least cost fuel in some power plants, this
is expected to change when oil refineries are upgraded to rationalize the product mix and surpluses of fuel
oil decline.4 In the residential market, gas is also expected to replace some of the current LPG
4/     Refineries in Russia are of a simple configuration, producing much less of the lighter products than the
market would dictate and an excessive proportion of fuel oil. With the liberalization of petroleum product
(continued...)



-7-
consumption.
3.5    Overall, the domestic energy market is expected to contract over the next three to five years
owing to: (a) the general economic decline in Russia; (b) a shift away from heavy industry towards a
more services-oriented economy; (c) new investments in more energy-efficient technology; and, (d)
investments in energy efficiency in existing industries. Many of these changes have started to occur as
a result of energy price adjustments, and the active role taken by the government in support of energy
efficiency investments. Gas consumption in Russia is forecast to decline by 20 to 30 percent from the
1992 levels as a result of restructuring and efficiency gains. This decrease could be offset to a degree,
however, if gas takes the place of other fuels in industrial and commercial markets.
3.6    As illustrated in Table 3-1, most of domestic gas consumption has been in electric utilities,
including combined heat and power plants (44 percent) and in boilers (19 percent). Use in transmission
is also substantial at 12 percent of the total. Gas consumption in the commercial and residential sectors
(7 percent) is relatively low because much of the space and water heating demand is met by district
heating facilities.  Consumption in households and many commercial enterprises is not metered.
Households are charged fixed rates based on norns which are related to the types and number of
appliances, the number of occupants, and the physical size of the dwelling. Unmetered commercial
consumers are charged based on type of activity and the equipment they operate. Normns are adjusted
periodically. Adjustments are proposed by the gas distribution companies (GDCs), based on the
difference between quantities received at the city gate and quantities billed to metered customers.
Changes to the norns, however, require local government approval prior to implementation.

3.7    Of the 48 million households in Russia, 43 percent (20.5 million households) are supplied with
4/(... .continued)
prices, refineries are expected to make investments to rationalize the product slate, thereby decreasing the fuel
oil available for power plants that would be replaced, in turn, by natural gas.



- 8 -
natural gas. Ninety percent of these are located in urban areas. Piped and bottled LPG are supplied to
3.3 and 13.3 million households, respectively, in areas where natural gas is not available.5 Residential
use of natural gas is mostly for cooking, while both space and water heating needs are generally met by
district heating, especially in urban areas. Average household consumption, at 865 cubic meters per year,
is therefore significantly lower than in other countries where gas is used directly for heating. However,
although no statistical information exists on the specific consumption, it appears that residential gas
consumption for cooking is about 300 m3/yr, that is, three to four times the usual figure of western
countries.6
3.8   In the absence of meters, the incentive for individual residential customers to reduce their
consumption to more acceptable levels is absent. At the same time, because the cost of excessive or
wasteful consumption is spread among all customers through the norms, customers who would prefer to
reduce their consumption, and hence their gas cost, are unable to do so. The lack of an incentive to
reduce consumption is further reinforced by heavily subsidized prices to residential customers, whose
monthly gas bill in mid-1994 averaged in the order of RR 144 (approximately US$0.07) - less than 1 %
of the minimum wage.
C.    Gas Balance
3.9   In general, the government has strongly supported gas exports both because of the higher
net-backs and the access to foreign exchange. As a result, gas is supply-constrained in Russia during the
peak winter months. This situation is expected to change considerably in the near to medium term as
both export and domestic markets evolve. Gas availability is expected to increase as a result of the
collapse in the markets of the FSU. This would lead to Russia's gas supply becoming demand-
constrained in the foreseeable future (see Annex 3-1), a situation that has already been manifested in the
summers of 1993 and 1994, when Gazprom was unable to find markets for gas that was available for
production. Hence, it is expected that many of the gas-supply projects that are planned for the late 1990s
will be deferred, and Gazprom will start to develop enhanced supply to the domestic market, including
gas storage to help meet winter peaks.
D.    Institutional Setting
3.10   Gazprom: Gazprom dominates the natural gas industry in Russia. Its monopoly on non-associated
gas production, gas transmission, storage and gas exports makes it the largest gas company in the world.
In addition, through the system of gas allocation, it strongly influences the Russian gas market. The scale
of Gazprom's operations, its financial strength, its control over domestic gas supply, and its access to
'oreign currency, combine to make it a dominant entity within the energy sector. Its contribution to
economic stability in a turbulent period of economic restructuring is an important and well recognized
factor. Through the range of its activities, and its direct access to the Council of Ministers, Gazprom
is able to strongly influence sectoral national policy, encroaching significantly on the role of the Ministry
of Fuel and Energy and probably other branches of government as well.
3.11   Gazprom was formed in 1991 as a state concern when the former ministry structure was
dismantled and the major branches of the energy sector were formed into separate entities with greater
5/     Does not include Moscow and St. Petersburg.
6/     In Western Europe, specific gas consumption for cooking averages between 70 and 100 m3/yr.



- 9 -
autonomy from the government. Although Gazprom has experienced two major changes in its reporting
structure, it has retained virtually the same internal organizational structure. Gazprom is strongly
managed from the center, at least at the strategic and policy-making level. This may be explained in part
by the central control of the financial resources of the enterprise, including control of foreign currency
and funds for capital investment. Gazprom became a joint stock company in February 1993 and its
shareholding is evolving. The government was to retain a 40 percent ownership for at least three years.
The remaining 60 percent was to be issued to employees (15 percent), to ethnic minorities in the Yamal-
Nenetsk region (5 percent), to Rossgazifikazia (I percent), and sold through a check-auction to citizens
across the country (29 percent). Provision was also made for Gazprom to sell 10 percent directly to
investors, including foreigners, in order to raise cash for development.
3.12   Rosstroigazifikazia (Rossgaz): Rossgaz, like Gazprom, was established in 1991 as a state concern
when the previous ministry structure was disaggregated. It was responsible for gas distribution, acting
as the intermediary agent between Gazprom's gas transmission companies and industrial, commercial and
residential customers in the regions. Rossgaz operated through 71 gas distribution companies (Oblgas)
at the regional (Oblast) administrative level. Each Oblgas in turn operated through a number of city and
district gas companies (Gorgas and Mejraygas, respectively). Some of these companies, typically those
located in industrial cities, were jurisdictionally independent gas companies; most of the companies
located in small towns and rural areas were simply departments of the Oblgas, however.
3.13   Under the technical cooperation agreement with the government, the Bank was asked to finance
a study on the commercialization and privatization of the Russian gas industry. The results of the first
phase of this study, presented in Moscow in September 1992, recommended that Rossgaz be de-
monopolized into separate, independent gas distribution companies (GDCs) and that its contracting arm
be split off as an independent company. In December 1992, the government issued a decree setting this
process in motion (for details, see Annex 3-2). By March 1993, a regulation was established to define
the process. GDCs were given the option of either 40 percent or 51 percent employee ownership, while
the remainder of the shares were to be held by the federal, regional and local Governments based on a
formula included in the regulation. The first GDC joint stock companies (JSCs) were formed in June
1993; by December 1993, this process was almost complete. Effectively, the management and
operational responsibilities formerly executed by Rossgaz have been transferred to the new JSCs, leaving
Rossgaz with an as yet poorly defined coordinating role. Recently, however, a twinning arrangement was
established between Rossgaz and the American Gas Association which may be fruitful in establishing a
vision for its role in Russia's evolving gas sector.
E.    Development Plan
3.14   Development plans for the gas sector in Russia have changed considerably in the recent past as
a result of privatization and the financial health of gas utilities. As gas utilities are established as JSCs,
they have become much more careful with their proposed capital budgets, cutting back proposed
investments to address only urgent needs. Gazprom has limited financing for system expansion owing
to a shortage in funds. As a result of the contracting market, GDCs are able to focus on system
rehabilitation rather than expansion. In addition, GDCs are focusing on investments that would enable
them to make the transition to commercially operated businesses by installing metering and billing on the
basis of consumption. These investments are expected to ultimately free up gas for export by inducing
energy efficiency investments (from both the supplier and the end-user).
3.15   Gazprom currently envisions the need to develop the Yamal gas field and had requested Bank
assistance in financing this project. The Bank did not actively pursued this project on the basis that: (a)
gas markets in Russia are expected to continue to contract; (b) gas consumption in other FSU republics



- 10 -
has decreased considerably (particularly in the Ukraine); (c) flared gas and dome gas in oil fields could
increase gas supply by about 20 BCM/yr; (d) gas markets in Western Europe are expected to grow more
slowly, in the near term, than originally anticipated; and, (e) gas consumption in Gazprom's compressor
stations will decrease as they are replaced with more efficient units. Therefore, the Bank has proposed
an investment strategy that combines energy efficiency investments, rehabilitation of the gas networks,
and debottlenecking the existing export corridors to increase foreign exchange earnings. Gazprom is
actively exploring the possibility of establishing a major gas-export corridor through Belarus and Poland.
Because the development of the corridor would be a joint effort by entities in Poland, Belarus and Russia,
this route is expected to face fewer problems than those faced by the existing gas transit route through
Ukraine (although Gazprom is also actively pursuing investments in the Ukraine corridor). Together,
these investments could increase supply to Western Europe at a relatively low cost by debottlenecking
the existing pipeline system.
F.     Issues
3.16   There are four main issues that are central to the development of the gas sector within Russia,
and also to the success of the proposed project. These are: (a) the commitment on the part of the
government to an ongoing program of price increases; (b) the potential to increase the efficiency of gas
use in the country; (c) the format for regulation of the industry; and, (d) the ability of GDCs to function
as privatized corporate entities. Although these may be regarded as separate issues, in fact there are
strong interrelationships among them, as discussed below.
3.17   Pricing: Gas prices in Russia prior to 1990 enabled full cost recovery to the gas sector with the
exception of gas prices for households. As the exchange rate deteriorated and inflation rose rapidly, gas
price adjustments lagged. In January 1992, many prices were liberalized, but energy prices remained
heavily subsidized. During 1992, the price of many energy products were adjusted faster than inflation
with the notable exception of gas. However, gas prices caught up (in relative terms) to prices of other
fuels during 1993, with industrial prices at year-end nearly 20 times the level at the end of 1992, and
household prices about 7 times higher.
3.18   Since mid-1993, gas prices to industries have been adjusted monthly in line with domestic
inflation (see Table 3-2). Gas prices to households were theoretically to be increased every six months
with inflation. However, although several draft decrees were put forward to increase residential prices,
no adjustments were implemented until March, 1995, when Resolution 208 was adopted raising prices
by a factor of ten to RR 20,000/MCM (RR 16,260/MCM excluding VAT and special tax). At the same
time, the Government adopted a resolution which would devolve responsibility for setting residential gas
prices to the regional administrations. It is expected that this action will help to ensure more timely
adjustment of residential gas prices, and also to ensure that prices are more reflective of the cost
structures of the local gas distribution companies.
3.19   Given that gas is market-constrained (see para 3.9), the basis for pricing in the Russian market
should be the cost of supply. As the sector moves to commercial operations, it is recommended that
price-setting be based on financial principles, with full removal of input subsidies. (See Annex 3-3 for
a more detailed discussion of pricing principles.) As a result, gas prices to city gates would depend on
location because of the considerable component of gas transport in the total cost of supply. The cost of
supply to the city gates, assuming full cost recovery on the existing system at international cost levels,
is estimated to be about US$25-US$35/MCM. The incremental cost of supply to industries would add
about $3-5/MCM, putting estimated March 1995 gas prices to these customers at approximately two-
thirds of their target level based on current market exchange rates. Based on estimated purchasing power
parity exchange rates, the March gas prices to industrial customers are very close to their target level.
