3.20   Because the assets of the existing gas system were originally financed by budget transfers from
the government, most Russian gas companies are profitable at current prices. While recent residential
prices have failed to cover the financial costs of service, residential sales are typically a small part of the
GDCs' sales. Margins on industrial sales are generally sufficient to offset these losses. However,
because future investments will be financed on commercial terms, gas prices to households will need to
be increased to maintain financial viability. Gas prices to industries are expected to reflect the full cost
of supply by the end of 1995. Prices to residential customers are expected to be adjusted more gradually,
reflecting households' limited ability to pay. The current target is to reach full supply cost pricing to
households by 1998. This position is viewed as acceptable, as long as the gas utilities remain financially
viable without input subsidies. The Bank is now assisting the government to establish a safety net for
the poor that would ensure that they could afford at least a lifeline level of utilities. Once this has been
established, increases in gas prices to households can be accelerated.
3.21   Proposed financial covenants for the project reflect the principle of financial viability by requiring
that future investments are financed under commercial borrowing terms and through internal cash
generation. Returns on capital employed would also be adequate to attract equity participation by private
investors within two to three years. Given their current financial position, GDCs can be financially
viable with gas prices to industries reaching full cost by December 1995 and gas prices to households at
about one third of their estimated full cost of supply. The proposed energy pricing study (see para 6.8)
will address other details of gas pricing including seasonality, gas allocation, long-term gas contracts, spot
pricing, and interruptible supply contracts.



- 12 -
3.22   Efficiency of Energv Use: As noted earlier, the efficiency of energy use in Russia is extremely
low. As a consequence, energy which might either be sold into export markets or left in the ground to
serve future needs is consumed wastefully in domestic markets. Limited pipeline and storage capacities
are also engaged in serving domestic markets when they might be better directed toward exports. Energy
efficiency investments would also increase industry's ability to pay, thus helping to address a growing
interagency accounts receivable problem (see para 5.26). Finally, increased energy efficiency would help
mitigate the impact of energy use on the environment.
3.23   The first prerequisite for energy conservation, price adjustments, continue to take place. Current
energy prices have already made a number of energy efficiency investments attractive with payback
periods as low as a few months. The single biggest constraint impeding the implementation of these
investments is the availability of capital. Financial markets in Russia are in disarray with a limited equity
market and a modest capacity to meet local debt financing needs. Financial sector reform is being
addressed separately, and is expected to help mitigate some of these problems. However, studies have
indicated that energy efficiency investments are not made unless returns are exceptionally high (at least
40 percent and typically higher). As a result, projects that are both economically and financially
attractive in Russia are expected to languish unless a proactive initiative is undertaken to promote such
investments.  Therefore, it is recommended that the already established Russian Energy Savings
Foundation (RESF) be used to ensure that these projects are encouraged through a combination of studies,
project preparation, information dissemination, and assistance to the government in administering the
onlending under this project.
3.24   The structure and mandate of the RESF are outlined in paragraphs 5.7 to 5.11. Although the
agency is generally considered capable of acting to support energy efficiency investments, some
institutional upgrading and support would need to be undertaken to ensure the project's success (see para
6.26).
3.25   Regulatorv Structure: There is a natural tendency toward monopolistic agencies in the business
of gas transmission and distribution.  The high cost of entry, the economies of scale in system
development and operation, and the spatial characteristics of supply networks discourage free competition
for markets among alternative suppliers. Some competition may be introduced by opening supplier access
to transmission pipelines, and by allowing local distribution companies to negotiate purchase contracts
with these alternate suppliers. Within the city gates, however, interfuel competition is more likely to
occur than competition among different gas distributors.
3.26   Within this climate, regulation is important to protect customers from monopolistic pricing. It
is equally important to protect the transmission and distribution companies, with their large fixed-asset
bases, from pricing controls that might jeopardize their ability to earn an adequate rate of return to
maintain and expand their system, and also to protect them from predatory actions by competitors. The
government has taken initiatives in this regard, introducing a draft law on natural and state monopolies,
with the assistance of foreign consultants. The draft law addresses the need to regulate gas markets, from
both the federal and regional perspectives. The Government has agreed to review the appropriate legal
and regulatory framework for the gas distribution subsector as part of the proposed Energy Pricing and
Privatization Studies, and to prepare an action plan to improve the regulatory framework .
3.27   Of more immediate concern, from the perspective of the viability of the gas distribution
subsector, is the issue of predatory competition. Over the past year, the gas transmission company
(Gazprom and its subsidiaries) has begun approaching some of the large customers that were being served
by local gas distributors and offering to provide direct supply. A number of customers have accepted
this offer, with serious repercussions to the profitability of the GDCs. If this practice persists, the GDCs
would lose a measure of their ability to cross-subsidize the unprofitable residential service through



- 13 -
revenues from industrial supply (see para 3.21). In addition, because the outlook for demand growth is
generally weak, the GDCs will be obliged to amortize their largely fixed costs over a smaller number of
units sold, with adverse impacts on their financial performance. The Government's recent resolution to
devolve responsibility for setting residential gas prices to the local administrations (see para 3.19) will
provide some protection to the financial viability of the GDCs, since it will facilitate adjustment of
residential prices to reflect the actual cost structure and customer mix of the distribution company.
3.28   Corporatization and Privatization of the Gas Distribution Sector:  The rapid pace at which
Rossgaz has been subdivided into a number of JSCs is an encouraging beginning to the corporatization
and privatization of the gas distribution sector. The next major steps will include shifting the orientation
of the companies' management and operating philosophies toward customer service and profit and cost
management, and generating a stream of projected earnings sufficient to attract equity capital. Achieving
these objectives would allow the state to divest their shares in the companies at a price that adequately
compensates them for their past investments. The government has requested support for a study to
identify the next steps in the privatization process, with a view to the ultimate full privatization of the
GDCs. Funds for the study are included as part of the technical assistance component of this project (see
para 6.8).   The corporatization program  would be advanced under the project through the
twinning/training arrangements with a foreign GDC (see para 6.13).
IV. LESSONS LEARNED FROM EARLIER BANK INVOLVEMENT
A      Relevant Experience
4.1    The first Bank loan to the energy sector in Russia was the Oil Rehabilitation Project (Loan
number 3623-0, approved in 1993). It is too early to draw many general lessons from this loan with
regard to Bank involvement in the Russian energy sector. Within two months of the project being
declared effective, over 50 percent of the initial bids had been evaluated by the borrower. This was an
unusually fast response to procurement, and reflected the borrowers' commitment to the project and the
intensive effort on procurement during project preparation. However, since that time, numerous delays
have arisen, many of which can be attributed to the lack of Borrower experience with Bank financing and
international procurement, and to ongoing changes in the political and economic environment. As a
result, actual disbursement on the project has been slow with almost 90 percent undisbursed as of the end
of February 1995.
4.2    The Bank has approved loans for natural gas development in a number of other countries with
a view to increasing its supply and commercial utilization. Lending for gas projects has increased in the
recent past because of its low environmental impact.  Project success is largely a function of the
commercialization of the executing agency. Strategies for achieving this objective include the following:
*     separating the government's policy and regulatory role from the commercial role of enterprises
*     stimulating private sector interests in exploration and production of gas
*     mobilizing external financial resources
*     introducing a rational gas pricing policy.
4.3    The Bank has also financed energy efficiency investments, and provided support for the
development of energy efficiency programs in a number of countries: Peru, Portugal, Hungary, Tunisia,
Indonesia, Yugoslavia, and India. Hungary (1983, 1986, and 1989) is the closest model, where
financing was made available through the Energy Efficiency Office of the National Bank of Hungary
(which was set up under the first loan) to finance subprojects in energy efficiency. The first two loans



- 14 -
were generally successful because of the government's interest in promoting energy efficiency and
because of central control which facilitated implementation. The third project was less successful, in part
because the uncertain economic climate in Hungary during the transition period served to discourage
investments.
B      Lessons Learned
4.4    Although there is considerable scope for attracting equity investments for exploration and
development projects for oil, only limited foreign risk capital has been available for gas projects
worldwide, particularly where the gas is to be sold on the domestic market. As a result, as a lender of
last resort, the Bank has provided financing for gas transmission and distribution projects in a number
of countries.
4.5    Lessons learned from gas transmission and distribution projects and from energy efficiency
projects include the following:
*     The Bank often limits itself to the introduction of covenants to ensure the financial viability of
transmission and distribution companies. Poor pricing policies, however, have also led to a lack
of counterpart funds, delaying the implementation of some projects. Rational pricing policy
targets for natural gas pricing and indexation formulas should be agreed as conditionality up
front.
*      A strong government commitment to institutional change is essential. A long-term view is
needed, given that all of the goals of institutional development will not be achieved within the
time span of one project.
*      Experience also shows that project implementation hinges on the availability of a well-organized
project unit to act as a counterpart.
*      Experience from other projects has shown that implementation and procurement is improved
when the number of institutions involved in a project are minimized and definitions of project
components are precise.
*      Training should be approached from the perspective of the entire institution so that the quality
is enhanced all round and the benefits are sustained.
*      Implementation of energy efficiency projects has been mixed, but appears to have required
incentives or impetus beyond that of simple a-ailability of commercial funds.
4.6    These lessons have been incorporated into the current project. The Bank has established ongoing
policy dialogue with the government regarding pricing and institutional reform. Although energy price
adjustments lagged early on, considerable adjustment has taken place since the beginning of 1993. The
government's commitment to reforms has been demonstrated both on the pricing issue and on institutional
reform, with gas distribution becoming demonopolized and joint stock companies established. The
employees of Volgograd Gorgas, one of the beneficiaries under the project, have elected for a 51 percent
ownership stake, indicating their resolve to make their company profitable. At the same time, Volgograd
Gorgas has demonstrated an awareness of the risks and responsibilities inherent in the project, and a
commitment to proceeding quickly.  Training for their staff will be accomplished through a
training/twinning arrangement, established through co-financing from bilateral resources. This training
will address all aspects of corporate management and operations. Finally, past failure to disburse lines



- 15 -
of credit for energy efficiency investments is being addressed by pre-identifying and reviewing a number
of subprojects, by offering competitive financing terms, and by involving the government agency
responsible for energy efficiency in the implementation process.
V. INSTITUTIONS
A.    Organization and Management
5 1    The general organization and management of Volgograd Gorgas, and of the Russian Energy
Savings Foundation (RESF) which would administer the energy efficiency onlending process, are outlined
in the following sections. Additional detail is provided in Annex 5-1.
Volgograd Gorgas
5.2    Volgograd Gorgas distributes natural gas and LPG to customers in the city of Volgograd, a city
of approximately 1 million persons located 900 km southeast of Moscow. The company delivers
approximately 3.6 BCM of gas annually, with more than 90 percent being consumed by industrial plants
(75 percent of sales go to 32 large industrial customers). Typically, a Gorgas sells up to 50 percent of
its gas for power generation, but most of the electricity in the Volgograd area is supplied by large
hydroelectric generating stations and therefore the Gorgas does not have a large power plant in its
customer base.
5.3    Day-to-day management of the organization is directed by a Management Committee consisting
of the general director, the chief engineer, the deputy general director in charge of procurement, and the
heads of planning, accounting and gas distribution and payment. The company has approximately 1,000
employees. These workers are now among the best paid in the city, and there are no vacancies and
limited turnover. The relatively high pay levels have also enabled the company to recruit qualified
graduates in engineering and other disciplines; this had not been possible in the past.
5.4    Prior to September 1993, gas distribution in the region was the responsibility of Volgograd
Oblgas, which operated through 17 subregional operating groups. Each of these operating groups chose
to become a separate independent JSC under privatization. The largest unit, Volgograd Gorgas,
submitted its application for JSC status along with the others in September 1993. Although the federal
authorities approved the application almost immediately, there was a long delay before final approval was
given by the local authorities. Local approval was given in early April 1994 and registration was
completed on April 14th. Employees hold 51 percent of the shares of the company, with the remaining
49 percent of shares allocated to federal (29 percent) and municipal (20 percent) authorities. The rights
of the shares allocated to the federal authorities will be passed first to the local GKI (Goskomimuschestvo
- State Committee for Management of State Property) and then to the Oblgas.
5.5    The initial Board of Directors of the joint stock company consists of the general director as
chairman, a representative of the municipality, the general director of the Oblgas representing the block
of federal shares owned by GKM, an elected member of the Duma and an employee representative. The
shareholders are to elect a new board at a general meeting to be held within twelve months of becoming
privatized. No consideration has yet been given to future dividend policy although senior management
believes that the best policy would be to reinvest all profits for the first three years.
5.6    The Gorgas's senior management is generally competent, positive, forward-looking and attuned
to future demands and challenges. British Gas has discussed with the senior management the main areas



- 16 -
in which a twinning/training arrangement would assist its future development, and has taken the initial
steps in implementing the twinning relationship. The management is enthusiastic about such a scheme,
and the Gorgas is likely to benefit substantially from the opportunity to expand its perspective. Details
of the expected scope of the twinning arrangement are set out in Annex 6-2.
Russia Energy Savings Foundation
5.7    The Russian Non-budgetary Inter-branch Energy Saving Foundation (RESF) was established under
Order 107a of the Ministry of Fuel and Power, dated August 3, 1992, pursuant to the Russian
Govemment Decree number 371 on energy conservation, dated June 1, 1992. The agency is intended
to operate at both the regional and federal level, through Regional Non-budgetary Energy Saving Funds
as well as through the Federal Non-budgetary Foundation. Copies of the organization's charter and
regulation are attached as Annex 5-1.
5.8    The mandate of the organization is to assist in implementing the government's policies on energy
efficiency, specifically addressing issues that affect multiple sectors. Its envisioned activities include:
(a) financing scientific studies, drafting of norms and regulations, and working on standardization of
energy consumption; (b) providing equity financing to assist in implementation of energy efficiency
measures; (c) providing financial support to the development of the manufacture of energy saving and
energy efficient equipment and materials; and, (d) providing preferred credit to small and medium energy
saving projects which meet specified technical and economic criteria.
5.9    The organization of the RESF is shown in Annex 5-1. The management group includes the
chairman, two vice-presidents (of technology and economics) and four department heads (of projects and
appraisals, regional programs, loans and finance, and external relations). There are also 11 sector
branches which focus on the needs of specific sectors or functions. Total staff complement is about 35,
of whom 25 are full time and the remainder perform their functions on a part time basis along with
regular jobs in the various ministries. There are also approximately 30 consultants or "experts" working
under contract. In addition, 12 regional centers have been established, consistent with the concept of
regional energy savings funds. The latter are independent of the RESF, but have a representative on the
RESF Board. The Board of Directors also consists of the chairman of the RESF, the vice-presidents and
the department heads.
5.10   Overall control and direction is provided by a Council of Observers, which is composed of
representatives from the Ministries of Economy, Finance, Science and Fuels and Energy. The Council
is intended to endorse basic strategy and policy, and audit one or two major projects per year to ensure
that the RESF meets its objectives.
5.11   Originally, the RESF was to be financially independent of the budget, with funding coming from
a number of sources including special taxes, fines and other assessments. The legislation to implement
the various funding mechanisms for the agency was introduced in the Duma, but was left outstanding
when the Duma was dissolved in late September 1993. At present, the future of the legislation is in
doubt. In the interim, funding has come mainly from the Ministry of Fuel and Energy and other
ministries. In 1993, the RESF spent about RR 0.5 billion, primarily on research and development. The
proposed 1994 budget is for RR 22 billion for both R&D and capital projects.' Current operating
expenses for staffing, consultants and overheads are RR 20 million/month (based on late 1993 salary
levels).
7/     The large increase in the R&D and capital budget is attributable in part to an eight-fold inflation during
1993, and also to increased commitment of funds from local and foreign sources.



- 17 -
B.    Legal Status
5.12   Volgograd Gorgas is a Russian Joint Stock company, with 51 percent of the shares held by
employees. The legal status of the RESF has been laid out in its charter. The Bank's legal staff has
reviewed this document, and found no barrier to the RESF's proposed role in implementing the project.
The proceeds of the loan designated for the energy efficiency investments would be on-lent directly from
the Ministry of Finance to the beneficiaries, with the RESF acting only in an administrative capacity.
C.    Accounting Systems
5.13   Russian accounting developed as an integral part of the Soviet economic system and is mandatory
in nature, as opposed to being generally accepted. All Russian enterprises utilize the national accounting
systems, which are based on a unified chart of accounts and specified rules for recording all transactions.
A new unified system of accounts was introduced in February 1992, and is being adopted by gas
distribution companies (GDCs).  This system, based partly on international Generally Accepted
Accounting Principles (GAAP) and arising from a United Nations-sponsored accounting review, is
considered to be an improvement over the previous unified system but still dictates the recording of all
financial transactions based on standardized rules and accounts and not on accounting principles.'
Accrual-based accounting was adopted in principle in 1992 and implementation is currently underway.
At present, however, accounting practices reflect a blend of accrual and cash-based principles.
5.14   Currently, balance sheets are similar to those prepared under international financial standards,
both in structure and in terms of continuity principles. Income statements, however, summarize profits
primarily for tax purposes, and also summarize the distribution of any surplus remaining at year end.
Because the Russian system recognizes revenues and expenses on a cash rather than on an accrual basis,
income statements reflect only the revenues and costs associated with transactions for which payment was
received. A more comprehensive outline of the total value and cost of goods sold during the accounting
period is contained in a separate statement on costs of production, which covers all costs (including
capital costs) incurred during the accounting period. These "parallel" statements more closely conform
to western-style accrual systems, although year-end adjustments to reflect prepayments and accruals are
rare.
5.15   Depreciation is computed and recorded on an individual item basis. Inventories also are recorded
item-by-item (materiality is not an issue under Russian accounting law). Maximum depreciation rates are
specified for various classes of assets. Assets were revalued at the end of 1992 to bring them to 1992
price levels, and again at the beginning of 1994 to bring them to 1993 prices. A similar revaluation has
been decreed to take place in early 1995. Revaluation is carried out only in response to a government
decree, which in turn stipulates the multipliers to be used for different classes of assets and different years
of acquisition. The revaluation in early 1994 raised the value of fixed assets of the gas distribution
companies by an average factor of about 20.
5.16   The accounting system  in Volgograd Gorgas generally provides an adequate structure for
8/     A number of other initiatives in general accounting reform are underway including (a) EU funding of
on-going work in accounting reform by the International Advisory Board (a group of Russian government,
academic and international experts), (b) UN sponsored translation of well known US accounting text books, (c)
the British Council's funding of training courses for 40 accounting trainers, and (d) World Bank funding of
training programs.



- 18 -
maintaining project accounts. Records are maintained locally, and the law requires that all transactions
be appropriately documented. The Gorgas is in the process of automating their accounting systems,
although the process is hindered by a lack of finances, trained staff, and appropriate software. Most
Russian accounting software is poorly designed, and with the volatility of the Russian chart of accounts,
quickly out of date. Thus, the company is developing its own purpose-built software.
5.17   The management infornation systems of Volgograd Gorgas will require improvement since they
have been geared to satisfying the information requirements of the ministries, and not those of internal
management or shareholders. Increasingly, the Gorgas will be required to make strategic decisions which
were previously made by the government. Capital budgeting procedures and criteria will need to be
developed and supported by the information system. Management cost accounting and cash management
will also be important factors which may require modification in the financial information system.
5.18   During discussions with Volgograd Gorgas, it was agreed that financial advisory services would
be provided as part of technical assistance to the project implementation team. Specialists would help
to ensure that project accounting procedures meet the Bank's requirements, provide short term advisory
services in critical areas such as cash management, assist in the preparation of financial statements which
conform to international standards and provide training in international accounting practice. The need
for computer hardware and software to assist them in meeting the financial reporting and control
requirements of the project were also discussed (see para 6.8).
5.19   A  longer term consideration, particularly associated with privatization, will be the need to
reestablish the financial position of Volgograd Gorgas. Current balance sheets are misleading for a
number of reasons. The historic cost basis and arbitrary revaluation of fixed assets results in the serious
understatement of the current economic value of some of the assets, and in the overstatement of the worth
of technologically outdated or obsolete items. The balance sheet also typically contains non-gas assets
(such as social infrastructure). Unrecoverable receivables and obsolete or over-valued assets currently
on the balance sheets will need to be written off. A physical inventory will be required as a prerequisite
to revaluation, with full valuation only possible once a stable fiscal and regulatory framework is
established. These issues will be addressed as part of the proposed privatization study (see para 6.8).
D.    External and Internal Audits
5.20   Enterprises are obliged to submit to the government quarterly income statements, balance sheets
and statements of value and cost of goods sold. Generally, gas distribution companies do not have formal
external audit arrangements.  External audits are only required in the case of foreign joint
venture/subsidiary operations, conmmercial banks, insurance companies, and investment funds. GDCs
are subject to annual tax inspections (compliance audits), however. Tax audits are adversarial and involve
checking that all transactions have been recorded in accordance with Russian law, as well as ensuring that
income, expenditures, depreciation and capital investments have been accurately reflected in the
calculation of corporate taxes due. Internal accounting controls are maintained through the internal audit
department within the GDCs, which typically have two to four staff. (Total accounting staff range from
8 to 18 people.) A typical GAAP audit based on Generally Accepted Auditing Standards (GAAS) is not
required, and it is not clear what it would entail at this time.
5.21   Currently, more than 2,000 firmns offer independent audit services in Russia, including the major
international firms. Many of the Russian firms, however, also engage in trading and other conmmercial
activities. A more realistic measure of those that solely provide accounting and auditing services would
be closer to 200. The international accounting firms are well established in Russia, some with a large
contingent of Russian accountants and auditors trained in GAAP. Most of the Russian audit firms only



- 19 -
provide accounting and audit services in accordance with Russian statutory requirements. As of January
1994, eight Russian firms have been accredited to audit project accounts on behalf of the Bank.
However, corporate audits based on GAAP are still the domain of the international accounting firms.
5.22   The audit firms in Russia currently operate without license or regulation. While there are a
number of associations of auditors, there is no authorized body responsible for licensing, setting
standards, or promoting the training and development of practitioners. In 1993, the Russian legislature
deliberated on draft legislation for auditing, which defined auditing independence and application,
prescribed licensing and regulatory arrangements, established auditors' rights and duties, and prescribed
the form of the auditor's report. The law was passed through the legislature but vetoed by the Executive
Branch. In its stead, a decree on Temporary Rules for Audit was issued, to become effective on
December 1, 1993. The decree covered much of the same ground but gave the executive branch
considerably more authority and oversight over the auditing industry.
5.23   Volgograd Gorgas has agreed to ensure that their accounts are subject to an annual independent
audit by an external auditor acceptable to the Bank. For purposes of project evaluation, the current
system of reporting and tax audits is considered acceptable. As the application of GAAS becomes more
widespread, the Gorgas will be expected to present its statements in this fashion. The proposed
twinning/training program should assist in this regard as financial staff gain experience in European and
North American utility accounting principles and practices.  Volgograd Gorgas has examined the
possibility of appointing external auditors, but at present, cost is a deterrent. As the audit industry
expands, and the distribution companies accumulate revenues, annual GAAP audits are likely to become
routine, and even mandated by the shareholders.
5.24   Reporting requirements were discussed with Volgograd Gorgas, and the company has agreed to
fiurnish to the Bank:
(a)    financial statements, within three months of the end of each fiscal year;
(b)    audited project accounts, beginning with the account for fiscal year 1995, within six
months of the end of each fiscal year;
(c)    audited financial statements (including a qualified restatement in accordance with GAAP
if required) showing their performance and position beginning with fiscal year 1995,
within six months of the end of each fiscal year; and,
(d)    by October 1 of every year, a copy of the budget for the next financial year, clearly
indicating how the financial targets can be met.
E.    Financial Situation and Prospects
5.25   Volgograd Gorgas was profitable in 1993 and, based on preliminary estimates, was also profitable
in 1994. The program of sustained increases in industrial gas prices, together with reasonable control
over cost of production, has enabled them to show net profits and a reasonable return on revalued assets.
Sunmmaries of financial performance in 1992 and 1993 are contained in the pro forma financial statements
in Annex 8-3. Data from earlier years have not been provided because of frequent changes in the chart
of accounts and recording requirements over recent years, and the distorting effects of inflation.
5.26   While the Gorgas performed satisfactorily in 1993, they experienced significant increases in
accounts receivable towards the latter part of the year, which led to corresponding increases in payables



- 20 -
and to reductions in the revenues recorded for tax purposes. The company's effectiveness in controlling
arrears has been less than ideal, with totals approaching the equivalent of three months sales. A listing
of possible steps to redress the accounts receivable problem has been provided to Volgograd Gorgas, and
management has been encouraged to act on these suggestions. Decree No. 307 (April 8, 1994) "on
increasing customer responsibility for payment for fuel and energy resources" also provides a strong
support for addressing the arrears problem, dictating that energy supply is to be curtailed and
subsequently cut off in the event of nonpayment (see Annex 5-2 for a copy of the decree).
5.27   Generally speaking, Volgograd Gorgas has been generous in their payments to employee-
shareholders. Excess payroll tax in 1993 (taxes payable on salaries in excess of four times the minimum
wage) amounted to 30 percent of wages.' In addition to paying high wages, the Gorgas distributed over
70 percent of its after-tax profits in the form of special payments and bonuses to employees. If the
company continues to maintain this rate of supplementary payment, its ability to finance local costs and
debt service under the project would be jeopardized. The company was alerted to this risk, and has since
undertaken initial discussions with their proposed twinning partner, British Gas, on dealing with this
issue.
5.28   Volgograd Gorgas had also adopted the undesirable practice of reducing its depreciation charges
in 1994 to 50 percent of the level allowable under accounting regulations. The rationale is that because
excess payroll taxes must be paid out of after-tax profits, there must be sufficient profits recorded to
cover these taxes. The company's projections indicate that if full depreciation were charged, profits
would not be sufficient. They therefore opted to reduce depreciation charges to maintain their current
wage levels. Analysis of projected revenues and expenses suggests that the company's concerns are
unwarranted. If they were valid, the preferred course of action would be to reduce salaries and/or staff
complements. Again, the company was alerted to the risks in this practice, and were advised that
financial performance would have to be recalculated based on conventional depreciation allowances to
establish compliance with the financial covenants.
5.29   Although the current financial position and outlook of Volgograd Gorgas is generally positive,
it is also apparent that outside agencies can have a significant impact on their financial prospects and
performance. A number of instances were cited in which the regional and/or municipal governments
regarded the profitability of local GDCs as an opportunity to improve the revenues flowing to the budget.
Thus, in some instances GDCs have been denied mandated subsidies (for example, to cover losses on the
distribution of LPG), assigned special "taxes", barred from cutting off non-paying customers, or
somewhat arbitrarily assessed higher corporate taxes and attendant fines. While tax legislation limits the
rates at which local governments are allowed to tax companies in their area, there are a number of ways
in which these governments can influence the companies' financial performance. Thefederal government,
and the Bank supervision missions, will need to monitor the regions' actions and the impacts on the
subsidiary borrowers, as part of its obligations under the loan agreement.
9/     As of 1994, excess payroll tax will be applied at a rate of 35 percent on that portion of salaries which
are greater than six times the minimum wage.



- 21 -
VI. THE PROJECT
A.    Project Objectives
6.1    The principal objectives of the project are: (a) to increase the efficiency of gas use within Russia
by upgrading and increasing the use of customer metering and by supporting investments in more efficient
equipment; (b) to reduce gas distribution costs, and maintain distribution system capacity and integrity,
through network rehabilitation; and, (c) to support the government's reform program through technical
assistance. The objectives will be realized both directly through project-financed investments and
indirectly through demonstration impacts.
6.2    More efficient use of gas will increase the availability of energy for export, either by increasing
the supply of exportable gas from existing fields, or by making gas available to replace domestic use of
other fuels, thereby freeing up other energy products for export. Increased gas exports will increase
financial flows to the government, which currently captures approximately 30 percent of the export value.
Increased energy efficiency will reduce the operating costs of domestic gas users, thus increasing their
competitiveness and potentially reducing the inter-agency arrears problem. Increasing the efficiency of
gas use will also have a beneficial effect on the environment, both directly through decreased emissions,
and indirectly by enabling increased substitution of gas for fuels that have a less favorable environmental
impact.
B.    Project Description
6.3    The project was prepared by Volgograd Gorgas and RESF, with the assistance of USAID-
financed consultants. The project includes three main components, each of which comprises several sub-
components. The components are: (a) rehabilitation of gas distribution systems; (b) energy efficiency
investments; and, (c) technical assistance to support the government's reform program and project
implementation. In addition, a companion Global Environment Facility (GEF) project will support
initiatives aimed at reducing greenhouse gas emissions including financing a portion of the leak detection
sub-component. The major project components are briefly described below.
6.4    Rehabilitation of gas distribution system:  This component will be implemented by the gas
distribution company in Volgograd and will include the following sub-components:
6.5    Metering: Under the project, the GDC will purchase approximately 48,000 meters. New meters
will be installed at unmetered commercial enterprises, households, and apartment blocks. Pilot schemes
undertaken elsewhere in Russia have reportedly resulted in decreases in gas consumption which range
from 25 to 50 percent. The replacement of outdated industrial and commercial meters will bring about
improved accuracy in recording consumption. Individual meters will be installed in houses that use gas
for space heating, water heating, and cooking, while commercial-type meters will be installed in
apartment-blocks to allow for billing groups of customers (typically, I meter will correspond to one riser,
i.e., about 20 to 30 customers).
6.6    Asset Preservation:   The preservation of the existing asset value will include five main
investments:  (a) pipeline replacement of about 10 km/yr, located mainly in older sections of the city
where pipelines were laid about 40 years ago; (b) cathodic protection of the entire system, because the
current cathodic protection system is generally ineffective; (c) leak detection and test equipment (partially
financed by GEF) for the detection of gas leaks, because the quality of existing equipment is inadequate;
(d) SCADA (supervisory control and data acquisition) system, to monitor system pressures and flows,



- 22 -
to initiate changes to match system requirements and to provide better information for operations and
investment planning; and, (e) network modeling tools, to carry out engineering analysis for system
capacity and design.
6.7    Energy Efficiency Investments:  The energy efficiency component will finance the foreign
currency portion of investments that are designed to improve energy efficiency for district heating and
power utilities. It will also provide assistance to end users in evaluating and implementing investments.
Because there are likely to be a large number of subprojects and borrowers, administration and
management of this component of the project will be handled by RESF with the assistance of consultants,
under the supervision of its board. RESF will be responsible for: (a) assisting potential executing
agencies in identifying subprojects and in presenting their technical, economic and financial viability;
(b) appraising energy efficiency investments by municipal utilities (district heating) and power plants and
advising the Ministry of Finance on the feasibility of financing these subprojects; and, (c) providing
assistance and disseminating information to industrial enterprises to evaluate and access commercial
funding for energy efficiency measures. The recommended investments for increased efficiency include,
inter alia: burner replacement and associated controls; boiler replacement at municipal district heating
companies; portable diagnostic instrumentation for power plants and municipal boilers so as to facilitate
better adjustment of the equipment;  automated control systems at boiler plants; and automatic
temperature control equipment and heat energy meters at heating substations.
6.8    Training and Technical Assistance: The project includes a number of technical assistance
components, which will provide support for project implementation, institutional strengthening, and
support for policy development. The technical assistance will include: (a) assisting Volgograd Gorgas
with project implementation; (b) providing ongoing training and support by establishing a twinning
arrangement with a foreign GDC; (c) providing training and technical assistance to RESF staff, focusing
on upgrading and expanding project analysis skills of staff, including investment appraisal and financial
planning and control (project financing, pro forma cash budgets, project monitoring and control); (d)
upgrading the current gas industry standards and construction procedures; (e) preparing a third party
access decree for the gas industry; (f) preparing specific tariff recommendations for gas transport; (g)
preparing recommendations for an energy pricing strategy; (h) preparing recommendations on the
privatization of the gas sector; (i) consulting assistance for a gas law and regulatory requirements; (j)
preparing an overall plan for gas distribution system rehabilitation; and, (k) support for co-ordination
of these studies. Draft terms of reference are included in Annex 6-2.
6.9    Global Enviromnent Facility: The GEF facility will complement this project by financing
studies on gas production, transmission, distribution and utilization to develop an investment program for
reductions in greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. Projects that would not otherwise be commercially
attractive will then be proposed for financing by the GEF. The study of the gas production and
transmission components will be carried out by Gazprom. The gas distribution component will relate
directly to this project and will provide leak detection equipment and consultants to assist in preparing
a program for methane leakage reduction. The gas utilization portion will also be undertaken in
conjunction with this project; it will include financing for emissions detection equipment, energy
efficiency investments (such as heat energy meters and insulation), and consultants to assist in the
implementation of the energy efficiency improvement program.
C.    Project Cost Estimates
6.10 Total Project Cost: The total project cost is estimated at US$131.4 million. Foreign currency
costs are estimated to be US$111.2 million (85 percent of the total). Table 6-1 summarizes the total
estimated project cost.

6.11   Rehabilitation of the Distribution System:  The estimated total cost of this component is
US$49.8 million, of which the foreign exchange component is US$36.9 million (74 percent of the total)
and the local cost is US$12.9 million (26 percent of the total). The cost estimate is detailed in Annex
6-3. The base cost of this component is estimated to be US$35.2 million (in mid-1993 dollars
equivalent), excluding taxes, duties, contingencies and implementation assistance. The addition of physical
and price contingencies increases this amount to US$46.5 million. Import duties (5 percent on imported
goods) and implementation assistance brings this total to US$49.8 million. VAT has been excluded from
the estimates.'
6.12   Investments in Energy Efficiency: The project feasibility study identified a sample of high-
return, fast-disbursing subprojects in Volgograd plus Ryazan, Saratov and Voronezh, that amounted to
US$50 million. Based on this estimate, US$58 million has been allocated for this component. During
appraisal, three or four subprojects were advanced in each city as examples that could form the basis of
10/    VAT has been excluded because VAT paid on capital assets is recaptured by the purchaser within six
months of the expenditure. In addition, imported equipment is now exempt from VAT.



- 24 -
other investments (see Annex 6-1). The total cost of this component is US$64.3 million, of which the
foreign exchange component is US$58 million (90 percent of the total) and the local costs are estimated
to be US$6.3 million (10 percent of the total). The total cost of the appraised sub-projects is US$19
million, of which the foreign exchange component is US$17 million (89 percent of the total) and the local
costs are US$2 million (11 percent of the total). Import duties (5 percent) apply to imported goods, and
VAT has been excluded from the estimates. It is proposed that the appraised projects identified would
be replicated in a number of cities. This approach would maximize the impact of the project and facilitate
implementation, while keeping costs low.
6.13  Technical Assistance: The estimated cost of the technical assistance components of the project
is US$15.5 million. Of this total, US$1.5 million, covering implementation assistance, is included in the
cost estimate for the system upgrading components of the project (para 6.24). Of the remaining US$14
million, an estimated US$2 million is the estimated cost of the twinning/training arrangement with an
international gas distribution company. An estimated US$2 million is proposed for technical support and
training of the RESF staff, while the remaining US$10 million will be used to finance the other
components of technical assistance outlined in paragraph 6.8.
6.14   Global Environment Facility: The total cost of the GEF component is US$3.7 million which
includes US$3.2 million provided by a global environment trust fund (GET) and US$0.5 million of
counterpart financing for services provided by local organizations to carry out field tests. Of the US$3.2
million GET grant, US$1.8 million will finance the investigation of GHG emissions from production and
transmission facilities, US$0.6 million will be allocated for studies relating to the gas distribution system
and US$0.8 million for the investigation and audit of energy efficiency investments. Further details are
provided in the GEF report No. P13076-RU. Table 6-1 indicates US$3.3 million as the total size of the
GEF component, as US$0.4 million has been allocated to finance a portion of leak detection equipment
of the asset preservation component.
D.    Financing Plan
6.15   The Bank loan to the Russian Federation will be in the amount of US$106.5 million, and will
cover the foreign currency component of the distribution system upgrading (US$36.5 million), the energy
efficiency investments (US$58 million), and the technical assistance components not covered by
co-financing (US$12 million). The loan will be on standard Bank terms with a maturity of 17 years,
including 5 years grace.
6.16   The proposed financing plan for the project is outlined in Table 6.2. Co-financing of US$1.5
million is being sought from various bilateral agencies to finance training for the GDC staff in the areas
of operations, finance and accounting, procurement and management. The training will be implemented
through a twinning arrangement with an international gas utility. Currently, the United Kingdom have
expressed a strong interest in twinning with Volgograd and have sent teams to work with the appraisal
mission to outline the program. The GEF will provide a US$3.2 million grant to develop an investment
program for reductions in greenhouse gas emissions. Volgograd Gorgas will finance US$13.4 million
equivalent from internal cash generation for local costs.
6.17   The funds for the gas distribution component will be onlent by the Russian Federation through
a subsidiary loan to Volgograd Gorgas. It is proposed that the government add 250 basis points to the
Bank's interest rate in onlending to the GDC to cover credit risks, cross currency risks, and
administrative costs incurred by the Government. Proposed terms for on-lending will be a maturity of
17 years, with 5 years grace to reflect the implementation period of the gas distribution component and
the cost recovery period for metering. Completion of a satisfactory subsidiary loan agreement between

the government and Volgograd Gorgas is a condition of loan effectiveness.

6.18   The funds for the proposed energy efficiency component will be onlent by the Government to
district heating and local power utilities, based on project review and appraisal carried out by the RESF.
Onlending for the sub-projects will be at 250 basis points above the Bank interest rate, with repayment
over 10 years, including 3 years grace. Subsidiary loan agreements will be established between the
government and the authorities who are financially responsible for energy efficiency investments. On-
lending will be in US dollars, with the beneficiaries carrying the ruble-US$ exchange rate risk and the
Government carrying the cross-currency risk. The Government has been asked to direct reflows accruing
from the differential loan terms to support energy efficiency investments. The Government has also
agreed to furnish the Subsidiary Loan Agreements for the energy efficiency subprojects for the Bank's
review prior to signing contracts under these components.
E.    Insurance
6.19   Insurance for goods and services to the point of delivery will be covered under the bids from
suppliers. Historically, insurance coverage for the assets and operations of the GDCs was provided
through budget transfers from the government. With privatization, risk has been effectively transferred
to the GDCs. Like most enterprises in the energy field, GDCs are relying on self insurance, although
there are no funds specifically set aside for this purpose. Instead, a portion of retained profits is set aside
in special funds for capital replacement and maintenance.
6.20   The potential to obtain insurance coverage against asset loss or liability is limited at the present
time. The Russian insurance industry is not well developed, and the more than 900 insurance firms are
generally small and inadequately capitalized. Only a few Russian insurance firms could potentially
provide coverage for the gas distribution systems, although they currently have limited experience in this
area. The cost of insurance coverage could range from 0.5 to 2.0 percent, depending on the state of the
facilities and the risks inherent in the operations. Premiums would likely tend on the higher end, given
the lack of experience and limited knowledge of the current state of the system.



- 26 -
6.21   The issue of asset and liability insurance was discussed with the GDC. It was agreed that the
GDC would continue to follow their current practice of retaining a portion of earnings as insurance
against loss and damages. When the new accounting legislation is implemented and the companies have
more discretion to set up special provisions, a portion of retained earnings will be transferred into an self-
insurance fund.
F.    Project Management and Implementation
6.22   The gas distribution system rehabilitation component of the project will be implemented over four
calendar years, starting in the third quarter of 1995 through the second quarter of 1999. All components
will be launched in the first year of the project. The proposed implementation schedule is shown in
Annex 6-4.
6.23   Gas Distribution Component: A project implementation team will be established in Volgograd
with staff possessing relevant qualifications. A project director will lead the team and will be supported
by a technical manager, a procurement manager, a finance manager and a logistics/administrative officer.
Considering the high educational level of the GDC senior staff and their understanding of the problems
faced by the companies, the preferred option will be to assign existing senior staff to the key positions,
with the chief engineer of the GDC taking the position of project director.
6.24   Considering the GDC's lack of experience with international and Bank procedures, especially with
regard to finance and procurement, it is proposed that consultant support services be provided. In
discussions with the GDC the following consultant skill mix is proposed:
(a)    Two consultants specializing in gas distribution will support the technical manager: the first, a
gas distribution specialist, with expertise in design and implementation of all aspects of gas distribution
networks will provide advice on project implementation and pipeline related designs. The other, with
expertise in metering design and specification, will be required in the early stages of the project.
(b)    A procurement specialist will advise the GDC in all aspects of the procurement process including
the preparation of tender documents, bid evaluations and contracts with suppliers of equipment and
services. The procurement specialist will also recommend institutional changes to strengthen the GDC's
capability to procure from local and international sources.
(c)    A financial analyst will assist the financial manager in establishing project accounting procedures,
in facilitating the preparation of financial statements that conform to international standards, and in
providing general financial advice to the GDC.
6.25   In view of the urgency, it is proposed that consultants be financed under the advance procurement
procedure. The cost for the specialists and support from their headquarters is estimated at US$1.5
million. The twinning arrangements proposed under the technical assistance component of the project
will complement the activities of the consultants. In view of the timing and the fact that the utilities
executing the twinning arrangements may also lack experience in Bank procedures, the twinning is not
seen, however, as a substitute for technical assistance.
6.26   Energy Efficiency Component: RESF will be responsible for evaluating, overseeing, and
administering onlending for the energy efficiency components of the project. During the first year, the
energy efficiency projects will be restricted to the four cities visited during appraisal plus St Petersburg.
In the following year, the geographic scope will be extended to include Nizhni-Novgorod, Samara,
Rostov-on-the-Don, Stavropol, and Vladimir. Pending the findings of a mid-term review, limits on the



- 27 -
geographic scope of the project could be lifted for the remaining years and for projects financed through
reflows. The responsibilities of RESF will include: (a) the review and approval of detailed sub-project
procurement needs (prepared by the district heating companies and local power plants); (b) review and
approval of the proposed execution of procurement activities, review of the economic and financial rates
of return and of the financial viability of the borrower; and (c) control and review of the technical and
financial aspects of sub-project implementation. Based on their review, the RESF staff will submit
qualifying sub-projects to a loan review committee, which will include the agency's board, supplemented
by representation from the Ministry of Finance. Initially, all projects will be submitted to the Bank for
final review and approval. It is expected, however, that once a base of experience has been developed,
the review committee will be authorized to approve projects requiring loans up to US$1 million. A
separate group will be established within the RESF to implement the energy efficiency component of the
project. During the initial phases of the project, RESF will be assisted in both project evaluation and
implementation by consultants to be financed under the institution strengthening component of technical
assistance.
6.27   Two sources of financing will be made available under the project to assist RESF: (a) the spread
between the Bank lending rate and the onlending rate will include 0.5 percent to cover their administrative
costs; and, (b) part of the proposed GEF project on greenhouse gas reduction will be used to finance the
costs of establishing this unit. It is proposed that the debt servicing payments by the executing agencies
be made as two separate components - one of 0.5 percent to RESF and the other to the Government,
excluding the 0.5 percent spread designed to cover RESF's administrative costs. This will provide an
incentive for RESF to undertake an effective follow-up on repayments. The RESF will subrnit cash flow
and income statements on a quarterly basis, detailing how their income was spent. Ministry of Finance
will be responsible for managing repayment of the subsidiary loans, with the assistance of an agency
bank.
6.28   RESF and the Government have agreed that the primary criteria for the viability of sub-projects
will be: (a) an economic rate of return of not less than 20 percent (real); (b) a financial rate of return
of not less than 10 percent (real); (c) a demonstrated ability on the part of the ultimate borrower to
finance the local portion of project costs from internally generated funds; (d) the technical feasibility of
the proposal; (e) the proposed implementation plan; 09 a debt service ratio of at least 1.2 to demonstrate
the ability, on the part of the borrower, to repay the loan to the Ministry of Finance; and, (g) an
environmental assessment which meets Russian requirements.
G.    Procurement
6.29   Table 6.3 summarizes the procurement approaches to be used for the project. The equipment and
services to be financed by the Bank consist of standard, conventional items. The meters, SCADA system,
cathodic protection, leak detection equipment, software and hardware for network modeling, and pipes
are available from a number of suppliers world-wide and will be procured through international
competitive bidding (ICB) except for some small items of less than US$100,000 and emergency purchases
which will be procured under International Shopping (IS). The Bank's standard bidding documents will
be used for all ICB packages. Equipment purchased for energy efficiency investments will also be
procured under ICB except for equipment of small value (such as portable diagnostic equipment) which
will be procured under international shopping. Construction works and equipment installation will be
performed by local contractors, hired using Local Shopping, and financed by the GDC. At least three
quotations will be obtained from local contractors and the contract will be awarded to the lowest evaluated
bidder. Works will be supervised jointly by local construction management teams and equipment supplier
representatives.



- 28 -
6.30   The consultants to provide implementation assistance will be selected and managed by the GDC.
It is proposed that this take place under advance procurement procedures. The training and technical
assistance to RESF will be selected and managed by RESF. The upgrading of gas industry standards and
construction procedures will be selected and managed by Rossgazifikazia, but with MOFE as the
borrower. With the exception of the twinning arrangement, the remaining consultants will be selected
and managed by the Main Department for Gas Industry and Gasification within the MOFE. Co-
ordination will be undertaken by the consultant selected for that purpose. All consultants will be selected
in accordance with Bank guidelines for the use of consultants, with the exception of the consultants for
the Third Party Access Study and the coordination. MOFE has requested that the consultants hired for
the first phase of the Third Party Access Study be retained for the second phase (valued at $700,000) on
a sole source basis. The consultants have performed satisfactorily and the Bank has no objection to this
proposal. The coordination of the studies requires that consultants be hired that are familiar with all of
the issues involved in the proposed studies. A consortium of consulting companies has worked with
MOFE in defining this program and hence are uniquely qualified for this role. The Bank has reviewed
the proposed consultants qualifications and has no objection to the government's proposal that this
component, valued at $300,000, be sole sourced. In order to accelerate project implementation,
Volgograd Gorgas has requested, and the Bank has agreed, to select consultants, satisfactory to the Bank,
on a sole source basis, to assist the Gorgas in the initial stages of implementation. These consultants will
be employed during the first three to four months of project implementation, at which time it is expected
that the selection of long term consultants under competitive bidding procedures can be completed. The
twinning company will be selected and managed by the GDC from proposals received from the bilateral
donors. The GEF component will be implemented and managed by the committee set up for that
purpose.
6.31  Interested suppliers from Russia will be encouraged to bid on the ICB packages, particularly for
the cathodic protection equipment, meters and pipes. The bidding documents for ICB procurement will
include the Bank's standard preference for domestic manufacture of 15 percent or the value of the import
duties and taxes whichever is less, so that eligible and qualified Russian manufacturers can benefit from
the preference, should they bid. This will include Russian and international joint ventures that meet
domestic value requirements. Considering the separable nature of most of the project components (only
network modeling and pipe replacement are inter-dependent), procurement arrangements will be
structured so as to be sufficiently flexible to accommodate adjustments in the work program during
project implementation.
6.32   Procurement for the gas distribution component of the project will be handled by Volgograd
Gorgas. The GDC staff will be responsible for preparing and maintaining detail procurement plans.
These plans will contain a detailed list of bidding documents to be issued and a schedule for each
proposed contract, giving the date when the draft will be ready for Bank review, when bid documents
will be released, the bid opening date, the date the bid evaluation report will be given to the Bank for its
review, the contract signing date, and the dates when equipment will be delivered or services rendered.
Equipment procurement has been structured in a phased manner to minimize the financial risks for the
borrower. This is feasible because of the divisible nature of the project. Periodic reviews of the financial
viability of the project components and the GDC will be undertaken in close concert with procurement
reviews, to ensure that the project components remain feasible. The procurement plan would be adjusted
accordingly. Annex 6-4 shows the preliminary procurement plan for the project.

6.33   All contracts in excess of US$100,000 for the gas distribution component will be subject to the
Bank's prior review procedure. All of the energy efficiency contracts will be subject to the Bank's prior
review. This will result in over 90 percent of the total value of Bank financed goods being subject to
review. If, during project supervision, the Executing Agencies are found to be undertaking procurement
in a satisfactory manner, the review requirement will be modified to exclude contracts of less than
US$1.0 million. The terms of reference for all consulting assignments will be subject to prior review
by the Bank. All consulting services contracts in excess of US$100,000 for firms and US$50,000 for
individuals will be subject to the Bank's prior review procedure. This relatively extensive review of the
procurement packages is considered necessary because of the inexperience of the implementing agencies.
In addition to the Bank's review procedure, a procurement specialist will assist the GDCs for at least the
first year when all the initial procurement packages will be prepared and issued. RESF staff will provide
procurement assistance to the entities responsible for energy efficiency investments. Selective post review
of awarded contracts below the threshold levels will be carried out on about one in four contracts.
H.    Disbursement

in fiscal year 1996, 38 percent in 1997, 26 percent in 1998, and 11 percent in 1999. This distribution
is consistent with the borrowers' ability to implement the project as determined during appraisal. While
the profile is more compressed than is typical for Bank projects in the gas sector, it is defensible based
on the steps which have been taken to accelerate project implementation." In the first year, the
disbursements will be for consultants to assist the GDCs in project implementation and for the first round
of items to be procured under ICB. In the second and third years, the remaining portions of the
engineered materials and equipment will be disbursed.

6.37   To facilitate payments of small and medium size amounts in foreign currency and all

I.    Environment
6.38  In accordance with Annex E of OD 4.01 relating to rehabilitation and maintenance projects,
the proposed project has been assigned Category B classification. The project will not require any
land acquisition for new, permanent right-of-way, and will not require any population resettlement.
6.39   The natural gas savings generated by the energy efficiency component will provide both direct
and indirect environmental benefits. The direct effects include reduction in acid and greenhouse gas
emissions (NOx and CO2) associated with lower consumption of natural gas. The indirect benefits
stem from the greater availability of natural gas and its potential substitution for more polluting fuels
such as coal and petroleum products. As shown in Table 6-6, the direct environmental benefits of the
erergy efficiency component in terms of reduced acid and greenhouse gas emissions amount to 2,000
tonnes and 1.2 million tonnes each year, respectively. The magnitude of indirect benefits, while no
less real, depends on the rate of interfuel substitution and has not been estimated.
6.40   An environmental mitigation plan has been prepared to address the potential project impacts,
and is described in Annex 6-5. Only three components of the gas distribution system rehabilitation
may have a measurable impact on the physical environment: (a) replacement of degraded pipe; (b)
cathodic protection (including new test equipment); and, (c) new construction, maintenance, and
operating equipment. Though the impacts of cathodic protection and new equipment will be
cumulative and not site-dependent, pipe replacement impacts will be dependent on the specific
characteristics of the sites involved. Mitigative measures, including the training of an environmental
coordinator, are described in Annex 6-5.

J.    Monitoring and Supervision
6.41   Owing to the nature of the project, the relative lack of experience on Bank projects in Russia and
the lack of experience of Volgograd Gorgas and the RESF with Bank procedures, reporting arrangements
are particularly important. These conditions will require frequent adjustment of the work program to
compensate for changing conditions during project implementation. To facilitate increased focus on the
project objectives during implementation, a set of key development indicators that will be specifically
addressed during supervision missions and periodic reports by the borrower has been established (see
Annex 6-6).



- 33 -
6.42   A project launch mission will take place early on to accelerate loan effectiveness and project
implementation. For the gas distribution component of the project, it is proposed that the Gorgas prepare
bi-monthly progress reports during the first year of implementation, then quarterly progress reports
thereafter. These reports will be furnished to the Bank and present the physical progress of the project,
procurement status, costs incurred and anticipated, disbursements made, administrative aspects and
institutional performance. A mid-term review will take place towards the end of the second year and the
Volgograd Gorgas will be required to prepare a report describing the physical progress and an assessment
of how well the project meets the overall project objectives and benefits. Because the project is broken
into a number of components, project supervision will require more resources than normal Bank projects.
Project supervision for the components to be implemented by Volgograd Gorgas will entail two
supervision missions annually. Requirements in terms of staff weeks are expected to be high (24 weeks)
in the first year, but will decline to 22 staff weeks in the second year, and to 20 in the third and fourth
years of the project. Supervision missions will also cover the energy efficiency components of the
project. Annex 6-7 details the proposed supervision program.
6.43   A significant monitoring and supervision effort is also planned for the energy efficiency
investment components of the project. Because of the number of sub-projects and enterprises involved
in the project, semi-annual missions are proposed during the first two years to monitor project progress.
The missions will include a technical specialist and a financial analyst specializing in energy-saving
projects. RESF, supported by the consultants providing institutional support to the group, will be
responsible for preparing documents for the Bank supervision missions. RESF will also be required to
submit to the Bank quarterly progress reports during project implementation, and to prepare and furnish
to the Bank, within six months of project completion, a completion report including a reassessment of
project costs and benefits.
6.44   During the implementation phase of the project, Volgograd Gorgas will furnish the following
financial reports to the Bank: (a) financial statements, within three months of the end of each fiscal year;
(b) audited project accounts, beginning with the account for fiscal year 1995, within six months of the
end of each fiscal year; (c) audited financial statements (including a qualified restatement in accordance
with GAAP, if required) showing their performance and position beginning with fiscal year 1995, within
six months of the end of each fiscal year; and, (d) by October 1 of every year, a copy of the budget for
the next financial year, clearly indicating how the financial targets can be met. The GDC will also
prepare and furnish to the Bank, within six months of project completion, a completion report including
a reassessment of project costs and benefits.
VII. ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION
A.    Projected Gas Demand
7.1    Projections of gas demand in Volgograd were based on a review of macro-economic and
demographic trends and the anticipated response of demand to the changing economic environment.12
The key assumptions in developing the projections are as follows:
*      Regional population growth will follow the growth factors projected for the country as
a whole. Changes in apartment stock will be directly linked to changes in population;
12/   In view of the considerable uncertainty as to the outcome of industrial restructuring, these projections
are regarded as general indicators at the city and regional levels.



- 34 -
*     Efficiency of residential and commercial gas use will improve as old equipment is
replaced and prices approach full cost recovery;
*     The number of commercial establishments is correlated to changes in GDP. In addition,
there will be extraordinary growth in the number of commercial establishments by the
year 2010 in response to growth in consumer demand, adding 50 percent to GDP-driven
growth in establishments;
*     Changes in industrial gas consumption are directly correlated to projected changes in
national industrial output (excluding fuels). In addition, modest improvements in
efficiency were projected as a result of higher gas prices and modernization of
equipment.
*     The areas served by the district heating system in each city will not change. New
housing units would be served directly by gas or electricity. Modest improvements in
efficiency are also projected as a result of gas price increases and equipment replacement.
7.2    Figure 7-1 shows the projected gas demand for Volgograd Gorgas. After an overall decline in
demand of approximately 6 percent from 1992 to 1995, gas demand is projected to recover to 1992 levels
by the end of the decade. By the end of the forecast period (2010), gas demand is projected to be
approximately 21 percent higher than in 1992.
