B.    Least-Cost Supply Analysis
7.3    An assessment was carried out of the long-run least cost means of providing space heating, hot
water and cooking services to new and existing residential and commercial customers and evaluated the



- 35 -
economically prudent limit for district heating. The following conclusions were drawn:
District Heating vs. Direct Use of Gas in Apartment Blocks: Energy losses in district heating
rise in proportion to the distance of the consumer from the district heating plant. The economic
limits of supplying space heating and hot water through a district heating system were reviewed,
as opposed to on-site heaters and boilers. While there are many variables, it was concluded that
the economic limits of centralized supply were within a piping distance of approximately 7 km
from a combined heat and power plant. It is estimated, however, that 90 percent of the
apartments currently served by the district heating system in Volgograd are within this radius.
The relative economics of maintaining centralized, versus shifting to decentralized, boilers would
be reviewed as part of the assessment of district heating subprojects, if relevant (that is, where
system expansion or upgrading of outlying lines was involved).
*     Gas Service for Cooking Only: In the majority of apartment houses served by district heating,
gas is used only for cooking purposes, and a major part of the low-pressure distribution system
could be closed down if electric stoves were used instead of gas cookers. The long run marginal
cost (LRMC) of electricity for residential consumers has been estimated to be roughly US$
0.09/kWh (US$ 931/MCM equivalent). This may be less than the long-run cost of gas supply
for cooking only, particularly when the cost of increased capacity of the gas distribution system
is included. Given the low projected growth in demand, gas system capacity should be sufficient
to meet growth for some time. Hence, the marginal cost of gas would be limited to the short run
variable cost of supply. This would be lower than the cost of switching to electric cooking.
Therefore, continued support for gas use for cooking only is justifiable until the gas network is
due to be replaced, at which time cooking loads would be more cost effectively supplied by
electricity.
*     Efficiency of energy use in apartment buildings is extremely low and could be improved
considerably at relatively low cost. In general, efficiency improvements are a low-cost source
of incremental supply.
C.    Project Justification and Benefits
7.4    For the purpose of evaluating economic rates of return, the proposed project can be subdivided
into the following three main components:
(a)    installation and upgrading of meters;
(b)    investments in asset preservation and control; and,
(c)    energy efficiency investments for district heating and power plants.
7.5    Capital costs of the proposed project components were taken directly from the cost estimates
presented in Chapter 6, excluding taxes, duties, and contingencies. Financial costs were considered to
be a reasonable measure of the economic cost of resources used; and thus adjustments were not required.
Probable impacts of the project components on operating and maintenance costs were reviewed, and were
deemed on balance to be negligible. Although some aspects of the project would lead to higher
operations and maintenance costs, other aspects yielded corresponding savings.
7.6    The benefits of the metering and energy efficiency components of the project were measured in
terms of the value of gas savings. The benefits of the control and asset preservation component were
primarily based on deferred pipeline replacement. In addition, the reduction of stray currents from the
cathodic protection system would decrease the deterioration of other steel pipe systems, for example,



- 36 -
district heating and water supply; however, these benefits were not quantified. Moreover, the project
would have important demonstration effects for cost-effective measures to improve the efficiency of end
use and of gas distribution in other cities in Russia. The economic returns are summarized in Table 7-1.
Subsequent paragraphs discuss the benefits analysis for each component, while Annex 7-1 presents the
detailed economic cash flow projections.
Table 7-1
Summary of Project Economic Returns and Investment Cost
Asset        Energy
Meters     Preservation    Efficiency    Total
Economic rate of return             59%           52%           25%         38%
Investment costs (excluding         20.6          14.6          44.6        79.8
contingency) (in US$ million)
7.7    Metering: Most of the meters currently in place for industrial (and some commercial) customers
were installed many years ago, and as such, they are likely under-recording consumption. Upgrading
industrial meters would alleviate this problem, either reducing the nontechnical losses of Volgograd
Gorgas or encouraging the customer to reduce consumption. The associated savings were estimated at
2 percent of industrial gas consumption.
7.8    At present, most residential customers, as well as a number of commercial enterprises, are billed
based on consumption norms, which are computed estimates of average consumption derived from the
total quantity of gas supplied. There is consequently no incentive to conserve gas, because the marginal
cost of consumption by the consumer is zero. The installation of metering for apartment buildings,
private homes, and unmetered commercial customers would link consumers' gas bills directly to actual
consumption. This, together with projected rises in gas prices, is expected to induce customers to use
gas more efficiently. The gas savings arising from residential and commercial metering are estimated
by the consultant to be equivalent to 25% of current consumption.
7.9    The value of gas savings was based on the estimated long run marginal cost (LRMC) of gas
supply to industrial (US$40/MCM), commercial (US$140/MCM) and residential customers
(US$140/MCM). LRMC was used as a proxy for benefits, rather than export equivalents, because of
the uncertainties associated with increased exports. If export markets are limited, gas savings would
enable gas investments to be deferred. The value of such deferrals would be the LRMC of supply. The
LRMC estimates reflect the cost of supplying gas to the city gate, the variable costs of distribution, and
the need for rehabilitation and expansion of the distribution network in each city. In most instances,
however, neither rehabilitation nor system expansion are a significant factor because of contracting
demand. Thus, operations and maintenance constitute the major part of the marginal costs over and
above the cost at the city gate under the current situation. The LRMC of supplying gas for cooking only
is estimated to be US$250/MCM, which is largely attributable to fixed customer service costs. However,
the value of potential gas savings associated with these customers is lower, and the benefits were valued
at the US$140/MCM level.
7.10   Upgrading of industrial meters shows the lowest rate of return at 10 percent, because the savings
are limited to more accurate readings. The introduction of meters for commercial and residential



- 37 -
customers shows an economic rate of at least 55 percent. Of these, the introduction of meters in
apartment blocks has the lowest rate of return (55 percent) because of the low gas consumption (cooking
only) and consequently small gas savings. However, the rate of return on the installation of meters for
commercial enterprises and private households are 459 percent and 140 percent, respectively.
7.11   Asset Preservation and Controls: In addition to meters, the gas distribution component of the
project includes cathodic protection, SCADA, leak detection equipment, inspection and testing equipment,
and network analysis tools. Each component provides a contribution to improve the long-run reliability,
safety, and economic and technical efficiency of the gas distribution system. The project benefits of these
components are evaluated as a package for preservation, control, and supervision of the entire distribution
system. Several components of the package contribute to the benefit. However, cathodic protection,
which represents the major share of the cost of the asset preservation and control component (57 percent),
also provides the main contribution to the economic benefits through the extension of the expected life
of pipelines, resulting in deferred pipeline replacement and reduced maintenance, and a reduction in
electricity consumption.
7.12   The value of the economic benefits is estimated as follows:
(a)    Deferred or avoided pipeline replacement is calculated as the opportunity cost of reduced annual
pipeline-replacement costs, because of an extension of the lifetime of the network from an
estimated 20 years to 60 years. The introduction of the SCADA system and network analysis
would reduce operational and maintenance costs. The effects of these components are estimated
to add 10 percent to the benefits of deferred pipeline replacement. The replacement cost of the
buried pipeline system is estimated at US$114 per meter (see Annex 7-1 for details).
(b)    The benefit associated with reduced electrical consumption by the cathodic protection system is
valued at the economic costs of electricity for industrial consumption: US$0.065/kWh.
7.13   Energy Efficiency Investments: The primary potential energy efficiency investments in district
heating and combined heat and power plants are burner and boiler replacement, meters and control
equipment, and instrumentation to improve oxygen trim. Considerable efficiency gain can also be
accomplished through better combustion design, modifications to permit operation as a closed system,
and controls and operating procedures in district heating plants. A number of subprojects, with a total
estimated cost of approximately US$42 million (at 1993 price levels), have been identified in the four
cities. The associated benefits of these subprojects have also been estimated in terms of projected annual
gas savings. Using a cost of US$40/MCM (that is, the LRMC of industrial and commercial consumption)
to measure the value of these benefits yields an average economic internal rate of return of 25 percent
on the identified subprojects. The project is structured, however, to allow a measure of flexibility in the
selection of subprojects, provided that they yield an economic rate of return of 20 percent or more. In
addition, by expanding the geographic scope of the project to encompass additional cities (see para 6.26),
it should be possible to replicate those projects at the higher end of the range of EIRRs, thereby
increasing both the quantities of gas saved and the average EIRR on the energy efficiency component.
In summary, the package of subprojects ultimately implemented could provide an average rate of return
that is greater than the 25 percent estimated above, but not less than the 20 percent minimum.
D.    Economnic Sensitivity Analyses
7.14   Based on the assumptions specified above, the economic rate of return of the project as a whole
is 38 percent. The following analysis shows the sensitivity of the EIRR to the underlying assumptions.



- 38 -
7.15   Project Cost: The cost of the investment program may prove to be higher than estimated by the
consultant. A 15 percent increase in project costs would reduce the economic rate of return to 36
percent.
7.16   Environmental Benefits: Environmental benefits of the project would result from a reduction in
the use of gas and methane leakage in the network. Reduction in gas consumption would reduce the
emissions of CO2, SO2, NO, and particulates. The reduction of CO2 emissions is valued at US$20/t of
CO2 emitted. If these environmental benefits are included, the economic rate of return would rise to 62
percent.
7.17   Exports and the Economic Value of Natural Gas: Gas savings in the industrial sector are valued
at US$40/MCM, and in the commercial and residential sectors at US$140/MCM. These are conservative
estimates of the value of gas saved, which reflect the marginal cost of supply in domestic markets (see
para 7.9). The medium- and long-term prospects for increased gas exports from Russia to Europe is
expected to rise (see para 3.3). At the time of project appraisal, negotiations of gas prices among
importers of gas in Western Europe and major exporters had settled at around US$80/MCM at the
importing country's border (at Waidhaus in the Czech Republic). This would result in a netback of
US$60/MCM at the Russian/Ukrainian border, or US$55/MCM at the city gate. If in the economic
analysis the opportunity cost of gas consumed by industrial, commercial and residential customers were
taken as equivalent, at the city gate, to this export-linked value, the benefits associated with gas savings
would rise to US$60/MCM for industrial consumption, and US$160/MCM for residential and commercial
consumption. At these values, the economic rate of return would rise to 48 percent.
VIII. FINANCIAL EVALUATION AND RISK ANALYSIS
A.    Projected Profitability and Rate of Return
8.1    Financial returns on the project, like the investments themselves, will be spread among a number
of beneficiaries. For Volgograd Gorgas, returns will accrue through decreases in unbilled supplies as
a result of improved and extended metering and through reduced or deferred asset replacement as a result
of network improvements. The associated capital costs incurred by the Gorgas are discussed in Chapter
VI, and include the costs of meters and meter-testing equipment, cathodic protection, network analysis
tools and related asset maintenance equipment. Retail prices of gas were assumed to increase in real
terms, reaching US$40/MCM equivalent (excluding VAT) by the end of 1995 for industrial and
commercial customers and reaching US$40/MCM by the end of 1997 for residential customers. Margins
were assuned to remain constant in percentage terms. '3
8.2    The overall financial rate of return to Volgograd Gorgas is 17.1 percent. Table 8-1 below shows
the rates of return separately for the metering and network improvement components of the project and
for the two components jointly. Details of the rate-of-return calculations are provided in Annex 8-1.
8.3    Clearly, the metering component of the project is not financially viable from the perspective of
13/    Margin refers to the difference between the price at which the GDC buys the gas from Gazprom and the
price at which it sells to its customers. Projected margins are based on a constant percentage spread between
purchase and selling prices. Because the margins must cover operating costs, interest, taxes, and return on assets
employed, it is the margin, rather than the absolute selling price of the gas, that determines financial profitability.



- 39 -
the Gorgas under the base assumptions with regard to prices. Although the potential economic benefits
are substantial in terms of induced conservation and consequent freeing up of gas for export, financial
returns to the gas distribution company, which are limited to reductions in unbilled supplies, are
insufficient to support the investment required. Possible approaches to resolving this problem include:
(a) recovering part and/or all of the meter costs through direct customer contributions; or (b) imposing
a demand charge on metered customers to recover the capital costs of the meters (including a return on
investment) over their economic life.

Volgograd           -12.3%                  34.9%                  17.1%
8.4    Under the first alternative, up-front customer contributions would have to cover a substantial
proportion of meter capital costs. To allow Volgograd Gorgas to achieve a minimum 10 percent FIRR
on the metering component of the project, customers would have to contribute 92 percent of meter capital
costs. Given the high unit costs for meters relative to income, an attempt to finance the meters through
up-front customer contributions is likely to be constrained by customer ability to pay. In addition,
substantial customer contributions could create uncertainties or disputes with regard to meter ownership
and, hence, maintenance.
8.5    Under the second alternative, Volgograd Gorgas would impose a demand charge or meter rental
charge that would recover the financial cost of the meters (including a 10 percent return on investment)
over a more extended period. Recovering the full cost of the meters over 10 years would require
monthly demand charges in the order of US$0.70 for apartment units, US$11.40 for commercial
customers, and US$230 for industrial users. If the Gorgas were to recover only those percentages of
meter cost required to yield an overall 10 percent return on the investment, demand charges would be
reduced accordingly.
8.6    The option of applying a meter rental charge was discussed with Volgograd Gorgas during
appraisal and was generally well received. The Gorgas management expressed some concern about
householders' ability, and incentive, to pay a meter rental charge given current economic conditions and
the extremely low cost of residential gas. However, given that the installation of residential meters is
generally concentrated in the later years of the project, by which time it is expected that both household
incomes and residential gas prices will have increased in real terms, the barriers associated with
affordability and incentive should no longer be an issue.
8.7    Assuming that rental charges are set to recover the full cost of meters and installation over a 10
year period, the FIRR on the metering component of the project would increase to 19.2 percent, and the
FIRR on the project as a whole would increase to 26.2 percent. Table 8-2 sunmmarizes the results by
component.
8.8    For the energy efficiency components of the project (those funds that would be directed towards
energy efficiency investments by local district heating and power utilities), positive projected financial



- 40 -
returns would be a condition of access to funds through the RESF. Returns cannot be defined until such
time as specific projects are fully evaluated and agreed to. The economic analysis, however, has
demonstrated that there are a number of potential efficiency initiatives with substantial returns. In turn,
RESF's activities would be financially self-sustaining, with the margin between borrowing and lending
costs set to recover their operating and administration costs.

B.    Financial Sensitivity Analysis
8.9    The major uncertainties about the financial profitability and rate of return on the project relate
to the distribution system rehabilitation components to be implemented by Volgograd Gorgas.
Uncertainty regarding the capital costs is a concern given the escalation in prices of local materials and
labor, and the ongoing shifts in the exchange rate. The financial analysis examined the impact of an
overall 15 percent real increase in the cost of project inputs. This increase would reduce the financial
rate of return on the network improvement components from 34.9 percent to 31.2 percent. At the same
time, however, changes in input costs would have an impact on the benefits of the network improvement
components of the project, which accrue from deferred asset replacement. If this associated impact of
a 15 percent cost increase is included in the analysis, the FIRR on the network improvements component
of the project would be unchanged at 34.9 percent. Because meter rental charges would be adjusted to
reflect actual capital costs incurred, higher capital costs would have only a minor impact on the FIRR of
the metering component.
8.10   There is also uncertainty about the benefits of the asset life extension program. The base case
analysis presumes that under the project the asset replacement period will increase from an average of
20 years to 60 years. Given the uncertainty about the existing condition of the pipe network, these
assumptions may overstate the benefits. Assuming instead that the average asset life is increased from
20 to 40 years, the FIRR on the network improvement components of the project declines from 34.9
to 28.2 percent, and the overall FIRR on the project would decline from 26.2 to 22.2 percent.
C.    Risk Analysis and Management
8.11   Background: Project risks were assessed to determine a risk mitigation strategy. The analysis
focused on financial risks instead of economic risks because of the relatively high project returns in
economic terms. Furthermore, as GDCs have become joint stock companies, support to their increasing
financial focus is important. The process involved establishing a list of project risks, followed by the
development of alternative risk mitigation strategies. The impact of the proposed mitigative measures
were evaluated to determine the incremental impact on expected financial returns for the project; thus,
a risk mitigation plan for the project could be developed. The approach is designed to increase the



- 41 -
expected project returns by developing a risk mitigation strategy in advance of project implementation.
This strategy would be adhered to during project implementation to manage risks.
8.12   Risk List: The uncertainty associated with the project's financial
returns, without risk mitigation, was analyzed using a monte carlo                 Risk List
simulation. Uncertainties associated with project costs (quantities and unit
costs) and benefits (quantities and values) were evaluated by assigning a      Equipment Quantity
probability distribution to each element and linking inherent dependencies.   Equipment UntCts

associated with costs justify the relatively high level of physical
contingencies.
8.13   Risk Mitigation: After the uncertainties were analyzed, a risk mitigation list was developed.
The risk mitigation proposals form the basis of a strategy in which risks could be controlled, thus
lowering the likelihood of decreased project returns. Many of these risk mitigation strategies have been
built into the project design concept, including: (a) conditioning the loan on establishing joint stock
companies; (b) establishing a twinning/training relationship to foster the transition to a privately owned
entity; and, (c) establishing a suitable skill mix within Volgograd Gorgas and providing outside support.
In addition, risk management would be actively controlled during project implementation, in particular,
during the procurement process. Three explicit controls would be enforced (see Annex 8-2 for details):
(a) actively promoting bids from Russian suppliers; (b) decreasing local costs by increasing productivity;
and, (c) critically reviewing bids to decrease the likelihood of low probability, high-cost purchases.

If bids prices exceed experience on prices for similar equipment proposed elsewhere, the process will be
reviewed and mitigative measures will be adopted (including re-bidding or, possibly, dropping
components from the project). As a result of the proposed risk control strategy, the expected returns
would increase by 1.7 percent for the asset life extension component and by 3.7 percent for the metering
component. The risk mitigation plan is detailed in Annex 8-2.
14/    The expected return is less than the most likely return because the uncertainties are negatively skewed,
that is, the probability of downside risk exceeds the probability of upside risks.

D.    Cash Flow and Financing Considerations
8.16   The ability of Volgograd Gorgas to meet its financial obligations under the project is contingent
on continued increases in internal cash generation as compared with past performance, the re-investment
of earnings to fund local portions of investment needs, and the maintenance of a cash-flow position to
meet debt service obligations.
8.17   It is proposed that the Gorgas would provide funding to cover all local expenditures. Given the
limited availability of local debt financing, these local components of the investment program would have
to be funded from internal sources. Meeting this requirement would necessitate ongoing increases in the
real levels of gas prices over the next 2 to 3 years (to approximately US$40/MCM equivalent), as well
as the retention of a significant portion of earnings surpluses over the period of investment. Real prices
of gas to industrial and commercial customers have increased substantially from mid-1993 (see para 3.18)
from US$8.80/MCM to an estimated US$24.19/MCM. In addition, on March 1, 1995, a resolution was
adopted to increase the residential gas price to RR 20,000/MCM (RR 16,260 excluding VAT and special
tax, or approximately US$3.70 at the current exchange rate), which more than doubled the real price
relative to the level at the time of the last price adjustment (see Table 3-2).  At the same time,
responsibility for setting residential gas prices was devolved to the regional administration who, as
guarantor of the subsidiary loan to Volgograd Gorgas, will have an ongoing commitment to maintain
prices at compensatory levels.
8.18   Analysis of the projected financial performance of Volgograd Gorgas indicates that the
combination of ongoing real increases in industrial/commercial gas prices, constant real residential gas
prices, meter rental charges (calculated based on a 10 year recovery and 10 percent rate of return), and
retention of surpluses would be more than sufficient to provide the internally generated funds necessary
to meet the local currency components of the projects, over the envisioned time frame for implementation
(see Annex 8-3). Over the longer term, cash flow is sufficient to meet debt service obligations and to
provide an average return on net revalued assets over the period 1998 to 2002  of approximately 20
percent. 5
8.19   Although the income and cash flow projections suggest that Volgograd Gorgas will be able to
satisfactorily meet the financial obligations associated with implementing the project, two provisions in
this regard should be noted. First, the analysis presumes that the company is able to maintain arrears
at not more than their December 1993 levels relative to sales. The experience of other Gorgases suggests
that this is feasible, and Volgograd has demonstrated a willingness to pursue the issue.
8.20   Second, the analysis grants first priority in retained earnings to the project under consideration.
Distribution of surpluses to employees and investments in other capital equipment are not included. The
projections indicate, however, that there is a significant surplus available in Volgograd Gorgas, after
meeting the commitments of the project. These residual funds would be available to meet other financing
needs and to be distributed to shareholders.
15/    The relatively high return on assets is attributable in large measure to the following: (a) the value of
the assets, which is based on historic Russian costs adjusted for inflation, is generally felt to be understated
owing to prior distortions in domestic prices; and, (b) many of the assets were put in place many years ago and
are largely depreciated.



- 43 -
E.    Financial Covenants
8.21   Proposed covenants are designed with three main objectives:  (a) ensuring that sufficient
counterpart funds are generated from revenues to cover operating costs and all of the local components
of the investment program; (b) ensuring that debt can be fully serviced with a reasonable margin; and
(c) providing returns that are sufficient to attract equity participation by the private sector.
8.22   A self-financing covenant would require the utility to demonstrate that they can finance the local
portion of the projects from internally generatedfunds. Local costs vary from year to year. On average,
local costs are 27 percent of total expenditures for Volgograd Gorgas, but during the first year of the
project, local costs are in the order of 20 percent of the total. In view of the Gorgas's need to build up
retained earnings, the self-financing requirement has been set at 20 percent for the first year of the project
(1996), rising to 30 percent for subsequent years. By the end of October of each calendar year,
Volgograd Gorgas would prepare, for the Bank's review and approval, cash flow analyses based on the
projected subsequent years 'prices, performance and investment program to demonstrate compliance with
this covenant.
8.23   An accounts receivable covenant is included in the loan agreement requiring Volgograd Gorgas
to maintain accounts receivable balances on trade accounts at less than or equal to the value of the
previous three months sales.
8.24   Once capital outlays are completed, the Gorgas would be required to earn an annual return on
net revalued fixed assets of 10 percent. This would allow a reasonable return to investors and ensure
ongoing access to equity capital. The company would also be required to demonstrate adequate debt
service coverage. Because leverage would be low, a debt-service coverage covenant of 1.5 is included
to ensure that there are adequate funds available to meet loan repayment requirements.
IX. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
Agreements Reached
9.1    During negotiations, the following agreements were reached. The Government agreed to: (i)
undertake a study that would address ways of improving the regulatory framework for the gas sector to
implement the Government's policies, particularly with regard to pricing and efficiency of supply, and,
(ii) prepare a satisfactory action plan to effect these improvements (see para 3.26)
9.2 The Government and Volgograd Gorgas agreed to the proposed financial covenants (a self-
financing ratio of at least 20 percent in the first year and at least 30 percent thereafter, a debt service ratio
of at least 1.5, accounts receivable not more than three months sales equivalent and a return on net
revalued fixed assets of no less than 10 percent). (see para 8.22, 8.23 and 8.24)
9.3 The Government and RESF agreed that the primary criteria for the viability of energy efficiency
sub-projects would be: (a) an economic rate of return of not less than 20 percent (real); (b) a financial
rate of return of not less than 10 percent (real); (c) a demonstrated ability on the part of the ultimate
borrower to finance the local portion of project costs from internally generated funds; (d) the technical
feasibility of the proposal; (e) a satisfactory implementation plan; (f) a debt service ratio of at least 1.2
to demonstrate the ability, on the part of the borrower, to repay the loan to the Ministry of Finance; and,
(g) an environmental assessment which meets Russian requirements. (see para 6.28)
9.4 Volgograd Gorgas agreed to furnish, by the end of October of each calendar year, a copy of the



- 44 -
cash flow analyses based on the prices, performance and investment program that demonstrate its ability
to meet the self-financing ratio covenant. (see para 8.22)
Actions to be Taken
9.5 The government would complete a subsidiary loan agreements with Volgograd Gorgas as a
condition of loan effectiveness. (see para 6.17)
9.6 Tendering for items where all or part of the acquisition could be deferred (for example, meters)
would be structured to allow phased procurement. This would enable a close linkage to be maintained
between compliance on financial issues and equipment procurement. (see para 6.32)
9.7  Finally, as part of its obligations under the financial covenants attached to the loan agreement, it
will be necessary for the government to monitor the actions of the municipal and regional governments
and agencies in the project areas to ensure that they do not take independent actions that would prejudice
the ability of the subsidiary borrowers to meet their financial obligations under the loan. (see para 5.29)

RUSSLAN FEDERATION
GAS DISTRIBUTION REHABILITATION AND ENERGY EFFICIENCY PROJECT
Russia's Gas Balance
Assumptions: Demand for Russian gas has been volatile over the recent past as a result of: the
economic reforms in Central Europe reducing energy demand; the economic slowdown of the early 1990s
in Western Europe; and, more recently, the breakup of the Former Soviet Union and the reforms which
have taken place there. These events have all led to a lower than expected consumption of Russian
produced gas. However, there have been recent signs of a turnaround in some of these markets.
Economic growth has accelerated in Western Europe. The reforms in some of the countries in Central
Europe are showing signs of success with a turnaround in economic growth. The economies of many
of the FSU republics lag those of Central Europe, as their reform programs have been slower in getting
started. For some economies (like Russia) this is expected to result in a similar turnaround in a few years
time. However, for some economies (like Ukraine) the delay in economic turnaround is assumed to take
longer.
Impact on Gas Demand: As a result of the above scenarios, gas demand in Western Europe
is expected to accelerate in the near- to medium-term as gas is expected to be the fuel of choice due to
cost and environmental considerations. The forecast assumes that Western Europe demand will grow at
about 3 percent per annum, reflecting modest economic growth and no policy shifts in energy use (in
particular, that a carbon tax is not introduced which would make gas a relatively more attractive fuel).
This forecast is generally consistent with those made by consultants outside the Bank. Demand for gas
in Central Europe is assumed to grow at about 4 percent per annum, on the basis that these economies
will grow faster during their period of economic recovery.
Demand in the FSU is assumed to respond in a manner similar to Centrai Europe. In the few
fast reforming economies, gas demand is expected to grow quickly after the shock of price increases have
been absorbed. In the case of Russia, demand is expected to drop quickly as bankrupt companies are
closed. This is expected to be followed by further decreases due to a change in the structure of the
economy and energy efficiency investments. The decreases in consumption are expected to continue until
1998 when demand is expected to grow again. For Russia's major export markets in the FSU, Ukraine
and Belarus, gas demand is assumed to drop until 1997, after which it is assumed to remain constant.
This does not reflect expected demand as much as it reflects the uncertainty with reform programs in
these two markets. The level assumed that demand contracts to a level that would be politically
acceptable.
Gas Production: Gas production in Russia is assumed to be a function of the size of the
available resources and Gazprom's ability to mobilize these resources. The production shown in the
attached table is a theoretical production level based on the utilization of existing fields and step-outs from
those fields. Hence, the potential surplus refers to the additional gas that could be supplied from existing
fields and step-outs if the demand necessitated it. The development of Yamal is nIot included as potential
production as it would not be a marginal resource. The size of the field is considerable, but the expected
cost of delivering gas to market from Yamal is expected to be about 50 percent greater than for existing
fields. Developing this resource would require considerable increases in gas prices which would, in turn,
invoke a demand response. Given the assumed level of demand for Russian gas, there does not appear
to be a need to develop Yamal in the near term.

EDICT No. 1559
OF THE PRESIDENT OF THE RUSSIAN FEDERATION
OF DECEMBER 8, 1992
Concerning the Re-Organization as Joint Stock Companies and Privatization of State
Enterprises, Associations and Organizations of the Gas Industry of the Russian Federation
In accordance with Edict No. 922 of the President of the Russian Federation "Concerning Special
Considerations for the Re-Organization of State Enterprises, Associations and Organizations of the Fuel
and Energy Complex as Joint Stock Companies" of August 14, 1992, I decree:
That the enterprises and associations involved in gas supply and the operation of the gas industry
which are enumerated in Appendix No. 1 and the industrial, construction and start-up enterprises
which are enumerated in Appendix No. 2 shall be re-organized as joint stock companies before
March 31, 1993.
The re-organization as joint stock companies of enterprises and associations involved in gas
supply and the operation of the gas industry shall be carried out in accordance with the procedure
established by Edict No. 721 of the President of the Russian Federation "Concerning Measures
for the Re-organization of State Enterprises and Voluntary Associations of State Enterprises as
Joint Stock Companies" of July 1, 1992, taking into account the special considerations which are
stipulated by this Edict.
2.    That the State Committee of the Russian Federation for the Administration of State Property,
prior to the re-organization as joint stock companies of State enterprises involved in gas supply
and the operation of the gas industry, which are part of associations, shall determine the shares
of the Russian Federation, regions and towns in the charter capital of the joint stock companies
which are formed.
The shares of regions and towns in the assets of State enterprises which are not part of
associations shall be determined by constituent republics of the Russian Federation, territories and
provinces on the basis of the assets which are transferred to them by the Russian Federation.
Those shares shall be valued on the basis of the principle that the assets of State manufacturing
and operating depots, gas-filling stations and high-pressure and medium-pressure gas distribution
networks, including installations thereon, are in federal ownership.
3.    That is shall be established that, upon the re-organization as joint stock companies of republican,
territorial, provincial and district associations involved in gas supply and the operation of the gas
industry, the ownership of federal assets which are recorded on the balance sheet of such
associations, with the exception of the assets which are enumerated in paragraph 3 of Article 2
of this Edict, shall be transferred to constituent republics of the Russian Federation, territories,
provinces, autonomous provinces and autonomous districts.



-49-
Annex 3-2
Page 2 of 4
4.    That, upon the re-organization as joint stock companies of the enterprises and associations which
are referred to in Appendix No. I to this Edict and of the State enterprise Rosstroigarifikatsiya,
members of the work collectives hall be granted the right to acquire sharers in those companies
on the conditions provided in alternatives 1 and 2 of Article 5.4 of the State Programme for the
Privatization of State and Municipal Enterprises in the Russian Federation in 1992 within the
limits of the value of the assets which are under the commercial jurisdiction of the state
enterprises and associations which are re-organized.
5.     That it shall be established that, when transferring shares to members of the work collectives of
joint stock companies which have been founded on the basis of State enterprises involved in gas
supply and the operation of the gas industry which did not incorporate independent enterprises,
the share of the Russian Federation may not be reduced by more than 20 per cent.
6.     That shareholdings belonging to the Russian Federation in joint stock companies involved in gas
supply and the operation of the gas industry which have been formed on the basis of State
enterprises which were part of associations shall be entered in the charter capital of republican,
territorial, provincial and district joint stock companies for gas supply and the operation of the
gas industry.
The interests of the Russian Federation in the Board of Directors of such companies shall be
represented by persons to be appointed by the State Committee of the Russian Federation for the
Administration of State Property on a submission from the Board of Directors of the joint stock
company Rosstroigarifikatsiya.
7.    That joint stock companies involved in gas supply and the operation of the gas industry may not
transfer ownership of shareholdings which are contributed to their charter capital by the Russian
Federation until the prohibition on the privatization of assets of the engineering infrastructure of
towns and regions and enterprises which operate, service and maintain such assets has been
cancelled.
8.    That the Russian State enterprise for the organization of gasification and gas supply
Rosstroigazifikatsiya shall be re-organized into the joint stock company Rosgarifikatsiya, which
shall be its legal successor.
9.    In order to provide for the development of long-term regional gas supply systems in conjunction
with the development of main gas pipelines and distribution networks, and with the object of
implementing a unified investment policy for the gasification of Russia upon the foundation of
the Russian joint stock company Gasprom, ownership of a portion of the shares belonging to the
Russian Federation shall be transferred to the joint stock company Rosgazifikatsiya with the right
to transfer them with the agreement of the Government of the Russian Federation. The shares
which are transferred shall be entered in the charter capital of the joint stock company
Rosgazifikatsiya.
10.   That 100 per cent of the shares belonging to the Russian Federation in the charter capital of the
joint stock company which has been formed on the basis of the State scientific research and
project institute Giproniigas and its branches shall be entered in the charter capital of the joint
stock company Rosgazifikatsiya.
The employees of that institute and its branches shall be granted the right to acquire shares in the



-50-
Annex 3-2
Page 3 of 4
joint stock company Rosgazifikatsiya on the conditions stipulated by alternatives I and 2 of
Article 5.4 of the State Programme for the Privatization of State and Municipal Enterprises in the
Russian Federation in 1992 within the limits of the value of the assets which are under the
commercial jurisdiction of the institute and its branches.
11.   That, in order to provide for the participation of constituent republics of the Russian Federation,
territories, provinces, autonomous provinces and autonomous districts in the administration of
the joint stock company Rosgazifikatsiya, the State Committee of the Russian Federation for the
Administration of State Property shall enter 40 per cent of the shares in that company which
belong to the Russian Federation as contribution to the charter capital of territorial joint stock
companies involved in gas supply and the operation of the gas industry in proportion to the size
of their charter capital.
12.   That it shall be established that dividends which are accrued on shares in the joint stock company
Rosgazifikatsiya which belong to the Russian Federation shall remain at the disposal of that
company for the first three years of its activity and shall be used to finance the technical re-
equipment, reconstruction and expansion of the production capacities of joint stock companies
involved in gas supply and the operation of the gas industry, and to carry out environmental
protection measures and scientific research and experimental design work.
13.   That the losses of joint stock companies which sell gas at regulated State prices shall be
subsidized from the republican budget of the Russian Federation and the corresponding budgets
of territories in proportion to their shares in the charter capital of those companies.
14.   That it shall be established that state representatives in the Board of Directors of the joint stock
company Rosgazifikatsiya shall be appointed by the Government of the Russian Federation. State
representatives in the Board of Directors shall form a collegium.
The joint stock company Rosgazifikatsiya shall present annual reports on its activities to the
Government of the Russian Federation.
15.   That the powers to control shares belonging to the Russian Federation in the joint stock
companies Gasprom, EEC Rossiya and Rosgazifikatsiya shall be assigned to the collegium of state
representatives in the Board of Directors of those companies.
16.   That the Government of Moscow shall assign premises of 1,000 square meters which are located
at Moscow, ul. Shcherbakovskaya, d. 41a and which are currently occupied by the State
enterprises Rosstroigazifikatsiya to the joint stock company Rossgazifikatsiya by way of legal
succession.
17.   This Edict shall come into force in accordance with the procedure established by the Decree of
the 5th Congress of People's Deputies of the Russian Federation of November 1, 1991
'Concerning Legal Guarantees for Economic Reforms".
President of the Russian Federation                                             B. Yeltsin

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
GAS DISTRIBUTION REHABILITATION AND
ENERGY EFFICIENCY PROJECT
GAS PRICING IN RUSSIA
Market Framework
1.    The principle upon which gas pricing recommendations are based is its market value. To
establish this, some understanding of the market for Russia gas production is necessary. Briefly, its
export markets to western Europe are about 60 BCM/year. This is forecast to grow at about 3% p.a.
Over the long term energy consumption in central Europe is still being rationalized, and demand has
generally been dropping with some notable exceptions -- gas demand has increased in Poland, Hungary
and the Czech Republic in 1993. Thus gas exports to central Europe are expected to remain constant
over the near term (as some countries pull out of their recession and growth starts), followed by a strong
increase in demand growth (to about 5% p.a.). Russian gas is expected to capture much of this
incremental demand as it is the least cost fuel for many markets. Exports to the FSU (particularly
Ukraine) are expected to contract considerably as prices increase and the structure of the economies
evolve.
2.    Russian demand is also expected to contract considerably, largely as a result of three phenomena:
(i) shutting down uneconomic plants and scaling back production due to market contraction; (ii) energy
conservation in industries that survive; and (iii) a change in the structure in the economy (away from
heavy industry and toward light industry and services) resulting in new investments using technologies
that are energy efficient.
Pricing Principles
3.    Industrial Sector (all but households): The reduction in gas consumption has resulted in a
situation in which gas is demand constrained. In a liberalized energy market, in which independent gas
producers would compete for market share, gas supply would compete for market share with gas from
other sources (rather than petroleum products). Under such a scenario, gas prices would approach the
cost of supply (as has been the case in United States) rather than petroleum product equivalents. Over
the near term, the short run marginal cost of supply would reflect maintenance investments (as expansion
needs are expected to be limited over the near- to medium-term) in the existing system, resulting in a low
marginal cost of supply.
4.    As gas companies in the FSU are privatized, the financial cost of supply has become the primary
consideration in price setting. These utilities need prices to be set at sufficient levels to enable them to
cover their operating and maintenance costs and most of their investment costs due to the linited access
to capital. To establish the appropriate pricing signals (to provide an incentive for energy efficiency
investments and to enable new investments in downstream sectors to be based on an economically
efficient mix of labor, capital and energy), gas prices should reflect the full cost of supply (based on a
reasonable rate of return on revalued assets). It is important that prices are not simply increased to this
level, as this could result in generating excess cash for gas utilities, which would likely result in
(unearned) dividends to the shareholders (resulting in an undesirable transfer of wealth from gas
consumers to gas company employees). Given that the Government budget was the source of financing
for these investments, it is appropriate that the returns on these investments should be directed to the
Government. This can be accomplished by anyone of a number of financial instruments including by
imposing an excise tax which will enable the Government to receive a return on its equity and thus help



-53-
Annex 3-3
Page 2 of 2
mitigate the fiscal deficit problem. This tax should be an interim measure that is gradually replaced by
corporate taxes as gas utilities start to replace the Government as the investor in the system's assets. Gas
prices would need to be increased to enable the gas utilities to finance part of their investments from
internally generated funds and to service a growing debt load. By taking Govermnent rent through
corporate taxes, incentives would also be established for corporate entities in the gas sector to improve
efficiency.
5.     In addition, gas should be sold at the "city gate" (or plant gate) at a price that reflects the cost
of extraction and delivery, and hence should be differentiated by region. Prices should also reflect the
seasonal variations in the cost of supply, explicitly valuing storage costs. (Gas supply during the winter
months is currently constrained by inadequate storage. Storage facilities need to be mobilized to enable
gas to capture more of the current market currently serviced by petroleum products.
6.     Using typical international gas equipment costs as a proxy to value Russia's gas system assets (as
the current book costs suffer numerous distortions), it has been estimated that the cost of supply to the
city gate in the Moscow region is about US$30 to US$35/MCM. Incremental supply costs to industries
in Russia have been much lower than other countries because the customer carries much of the
incremental investment costs. Hence, the total supply cost to industrial customers in the Moscow region
is estimated to be about US$35-US$40/MCM. Gas prices as of April 1995 are about 60-70 percent of
this target.
7.     Sales to Households: Gas prices for households would have to be increased by about 20-fold
(over the March 1995 levels) to reflect the full (replacement) cost of supply. However, since much of
the asset base is fully amortized and maintenance costs are low, March 1995 prices are estimated to cover
about 10% of the current cost of supply. With current income levels, increasing gas prices to fully
recover costs is clearly not feasible, since utility costs would exceed many households total income. This
problem can be addressed in one of three ways: (i) gas prices to households can be increased gradually,
while the difference between the financial cost of supply and the price charged to households is covered
by cross-subsidies from industrial consumption; (ii) gas prices to households could be increased gradually
as in (i), while the difference between the cost of supply and the price charged is explicitly identified and
financed from a Government budget transfer; or, (iii) gas prices could be increased to reflect the full cost
of supply and the customers would receive a cash transfer from the Government through a social safety
net to help pay for this increased cost.
8.     The advantage of the first option is that it is easy to implement and has a low administrative cost.
The disadvantage is that the incentive for households to conserve energy is relatively low, and that the
existing price distortion is addressed by introducing another price distortion. When this approach has
been used elsewhere, these distortions are usually retained for a longer period of time than is desirable.
The advantage of (ii) over (i) is that the price distortion to industries is avoided. However, experience
elsewhere indicates that these budget transfers are either slow in coming or aren't paid at all. (A good
example of this is the case of pensioners in Russia who receive gas at a 50 percent discount, while the
remaining 50 percent is expected to be paid by the municipality. Gas distribution companies complain
that this payment has not been forthcoming.) Option (ii) also suffers from an increased administrative
cost over option (i). The advantage of option (iii) is that all prices reflect their economic cost, providing
the consumer with appropriate incentives (households could profit considerably from adjusting their
consumption). However, it is administratively burdensome and difficult to implement. Therefore, the
recommended gas price strategy for households is to increase these prices gradually, while maintaining
financial viability for utilities through cross-subsidies if necessary. When a safety net is established, the
cross-subsidy should be removed and replaced by efficient gas prices to households.

CHARTER, REGULATION AND ORGANIZATION OF
THE RUSSIAN ENERGY SAVINGS FOUNDATION
Approved:
First deputy minister
of fuel and power generation
of the Russian Federation
A.E. Evtushenko
July 29, 1992
CHARTER
of the state enterprise
The Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation Board
at the Ministry of Fuel and Power Generation of the Russian Federation
The state enterprise The Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation
Board (hereinafter the "Foundation Board") is a component of the Ministry of Fuel and Power
Generation of the Russian Federation. The Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-
Saving Foundation Board was established by Order No. 107a of the Ministry of Fuel and
Power Generation of the Russian Federation, dated August 3, 1992, in pursuance of
Resolution No 371 of the Government of the Russian Federation, dated June 1, 1992.
2.    The Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation Board shall act in
conformity with the Law of the Russian Federation "On enterprises and entrepreneurial
activity", and Regulations on the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving
Foundation Board on the basis of cost-accounting; it shall have its own balance-sheet and
legal-entity status. The Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation
Board shall have a triangular stamp and a seal with the official emblem of the Russian
Federation and its name; it shall have settlement account No           with
; the Foundation Board shall be located at the following
address: 31 Ulitsa Gilyarovskogo, 129832 Moscow.
3.    The Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation Board shall be guided
in its operations by laws and decrees of the President of the Russian Federation, resolutions
and directives of the Government of the Russian Federation, ministered normative acts, and
this Charter.
4.    The Energy-Saving Foundation Board may create (reorganize and liquidate) subdivisions
(structural entities), subsidiaries, representative offices, branches and other affiliates which, in
turn, may open current accounts and settlement accounts; the Energy-Saving Foundation
Board shall endorse regulations governing operations of such entities. Joint ventures with
foreign partners may be set up if authorized by the Ministry of Fuel and Power Generation of



-55-
Annex 5-1
Page 2 of 12
the Russian Federation.
5.    Property of the Foundation Board shall include fixed assets, current assets and other valuables
recorded in a separate balance-sheet. It shall consist of the federal property of the Russian
Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation and the equity capital of the
enterprise. The sources of property of the Energy-Saving Foundation Board shall be the
federal property of the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation and
the equity capital of the enterprise. The sources of property of the Energy-Saving Foundation
Board shall be the federal property of the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-
Saving Foundation (to be formed in conformity with Regulations on the Foundation),
government investments, interest owned by other enterprises, profits from its operation and
other sources as envisaged by law. State property shall be assigned to the Foundation Board
for operation in keeping with relevant laws of the Russian Federation. Property acquired with
the employees' savings and the funds allocated out of profits for distribution among the
employees shall be used as stipulated by point III of the Resolution of the PSFSR Supreme
Soviet "On enforcement of the RSFSR Law "On property in the RSFSR".
The Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation Board may sell, assign,
exchange, lease, provide for temporary use at no charge and lend its property to other
enterprises; it may also write off, sell and lease its property to individual citizens.
6.    The statutory capital of the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation
Board shall accumulate allocations in conformity with Regulations on the Russian
Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation Board. The Foundation Board may
also establish such other funds as are permitted by law.
7.    Returns on capital of the Foundation shall be distributed as stipulated by the Regulations on
the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation. The Foundation Board
shall us revenues from its operations to build equity capital, with which it shall finance efforts
aimed at achieving its objectives. Distribution of profits from operations financed with the
equity capital of the enterprise shall be consistent with effective laws and other normative acts
of the government.
8.    The key objective of the Foundation Board shall consist in implementation at the inter-sectoral
level of the state energy-saving policy by means of financial, economic, legal, regulatory,
logistical and technical support. The scope of operation of the Foundation Board shall include
implementation of investment, financial, crediting and technical policies with respect to major
sectoral energy-saving projects and programs, and also commercial activity and business
intermediation, in conformity with relevant laws and requirements of the market economy.
In view of the above, the Foundation Board shall be entrusted with the following tasks
relating to management of the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving
Foundation    Board:
-      investment activity, jointly with ministries, other govermnental agencies and regions,
with regard to implementation of inter-sectoral and major sectoral energy-saving projects and
programs;
-     development of the material base of the energy-saving process to meet demand in the



-56-
Annex 5-1
Page 3 of 12
economy for energy-saving equipment and devices, and organization of the energy-saving
equipment market;
-      optimization of economic techniques of managing the energy-saving activities,
preparation of regulatory acts and energy-consumption standards;
-      assessment and selection in a competitive process of the most cost-effective and
commercially attractive energy-saving projects;
-     organization, jointly with regional bodies of state administration, of control over
collection of payments to the federal and regional energy-saving funds, use of the funds;
-      training and re-training of staff, including study trips to other countries;
-      implementation of foreign economic activity in keeping with relevant laws;
coordination of use of financial resources of international foundations to implement joint
energy-saving programs, including programs involving other CIS states.
The Foundation Board shall accumulate earnings to finance implementation of the following
objectives:
-      creation and operation of systems providing information backup and advertising
support of energy-saving undertakings, legal consultations and legal assessment of
inter-sectoral projects and programs, insurance of investments by commercial structures and
foreign parties in the energy-saving sector in Russia;
-      technical assistance to regional energy-saving authorities, enterprises and
organizations;
-      preparation of projects and programs, and their assessment by expert groups appointed
directly by the Foundation Board;
-      implementation of energy-saving projects under the management by the Foundation
Board, with financing through credit resources of the Foundation;
-      commercial activity and business intermediation consistently with objectives of the
Foundation.
For the purposes of implementing the above tasks, the Foundation Board shall be vested with
the following powers:
-     to engage in commercial activity in pursuance of the objectives;
-      to open representative offices outside the Russian Federation, create joint ventures and
organizations for solution of scientific, technological, manufacturing and economic issues of
energy-saving; perform transactions involving purchase of shares of foreign firms and
organizations and real estate on the territory of foreign countries;
-      to define it8 own personnel structure and that of structural subdivisions; to define the



-57-
Annex 5-1
Page 4 of 12
terms, conditions and arrangements for payment of wages, salaries and bonuses;
-     to finance the operating costs of the Board out of its profits and by means of
allocating 20 percent of income received in the form of interest payments. During the first 12
months of operation of the Foundation Board shall be financed by the Foundation, and the
budget of the Board for that period shall be subject to approval by the Supervisory Council of
the Ministry of Fuel and Power Generation of the Russian Federation.
9.    The Board of the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation shall be
headed by the Chairman of the Board (General Director) who shall be appointed by the
Ministry of Fuel and Power Generation in keeping with applicable regulations. The Chairman
of the Board shall be vested with the authority to take decisions on all matters concerning
operation of the Board; the Chairman of the Board shall be responsible to the Ministry as a
body of state administration for implementation of the key objectives, satisfaction of
government needs, compliance with standards of production, finance and economic
management, and for mobilization training and civil defense.
10.   Deputy general directors of the Foundation Board, chief officers of administrative and
structural subdivisions, and of affiliates shall be appointed and released from office by the
General Director of the Foundation Board. The scope of operation of the Foundation Board
shall include implementation of investment, financial, crediting and technical policies with
respect to major sectoral energy-saving projects and programs, and also commercial activity
and business intermediation, in conformity with relevant laws and requirements of the market
economy.
In view of the above, the Foundation Board shall be entrusted with the following tasks
relating to management of the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving
Foundation Board:
-     investment activity, jointly with ministries, other governmental agencies and regions,
with regard to implementation of inter-sectoral and major sectoral energy-saving projects and
programs;
-     development of the material base of the energy-saving process to meet demand in the
economy for energy-saving equipment and devices, and organization of the energy-saving
equipment market;
-     optimization of economic techniques of managing the energy-saving activities,
preparation of regulatory acts and energy-consumption standards;
-     assessment and selection in a competitive process of the most cost-effective and
commercially attractive energy-saving projects;
-     organization, jointly with regional bodies of state administration, of control over
collection of payments the federal and regional energy-saving funds, use of funds;
-     training and re-training of staff, including study trips to other countries;
-     implementation of foreign economic activity in keeping with relevant laws;

Annex 5-1
Page 5 of 12
coordination of use of financial resources of international foundations to implement joint
energy-saving programs, including programs involving other CIS states.
The Foundation Board shall accumulate earnings to finance implementation of the following
objectives:
-      creation and operation of systems providing information backup and advertising
support of energy-saving undertakings, legal consultations and legal assessment of
inter-sectoral projects and programs, insurance of investments by commercial structures and
foreign parties in the energy-saving sector in Russia;
-     technical assistance to regional energy-saving authorities, enterprises and
organizations;
11.   The General Director of the Foundation Board shall be vested with the power to approve the
structure and staff of the Russian Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation
Board, regulations on structural subdivisions and affiliates; the General Director shall enjoy
the authority to appoint and dismiss employees of the Foundation Board, pay bonuses, and
impose disciplinary sanctions on employees in keeping with the relevant procedure.
12.   The employees of the Foundation Board shall exercise their rights primarily through the
employees' meeting. The scope of authority of the employees' meeting shall be as follows:
-      review and amendment of the Charter of the Foundation Board, subject to approval by
the Ministry of Fuel and Power Generation of the Russian Federation;
decisions on creation, reorganization and liquidation of affiliates;
decisions on accession to, and secession from, voluntary associations;
-      other matters stipulated in the Law of the Russian Federation "On enterprises and
entrepreneurial activity".
13.   The Foundation Board shall elect a council of the work collective for a term of 3 years. The
council shall include the General Director, chief officers and representatives of work
collectives of structural subdivisions or affiliates. The council shall take decisions by majority
of vote provided there is a quorum of at least two thirds of its members.
The council of the work collective shall deal with the following:
define a development strategy of the enterprise;
-      review other matters as envisaged in regulations approved by the meeting.
14.   All documents authorizing payment or acceptance of money or other valuables, or dealing
with payments and lending to enterprises and organization on terms subject to modification,
shall be signed by the General Director (the first deputy general director in the absence of the
general director) and the Chief Accountant of the Foundation Board.

Annex 5-1
Page 6 of 12
15.   Control over operation and storage of state property of the Foundation Fund shall be
exercised by the Ministry of Fuel and Power Generation or in the rnanner defined by the
ministry, through comprehensive audits of operations, and by financial and banking
institutions within their scope of authority.
16.   The Ministry shall form a Supervisory Council consistently with regulations on the
Foundation, to coordinate investments and financial operations of the Foundation, to
coordinate investments and financial operations of the Foundation Board, and to define a
strategy for implementation of major inter-sectoral projects and programs. Composition of
the Council shall be agreed with relevant organizations of the Ministry of Fuel and Power
Generation of the Russian Federation.
The Council shall include eminent researchers and experts, officials of the Ministry of Fuel
and Power Generation of the Russian Federation and of other ministries, the Foundation
Board, regional energy-saving sector with materials or equipment.
The order of priority, contents and effectiveness of research shall be judged by the
inter-sectoral Scientific-Technical Council attached to the Russian Extra-budgetary
Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation Board.
17.    Liquidation and reorganization (merger, acquisition, break-up, transformation) of the Russian
Extra-budgetary Inter-sectoral Energy-Saving Foundation board shall be governed by relevant
legislative acts of the Russian Federation.
