REGULATION
of the Russian extra-budgetary interindustrial
energy saving fund
General provisions
1.    The Russian extra-budgetary interindustrial energy saving fund hereinafter referred to
as the Energy Saving Fund is set up in compliance with the Regulation of the Government of the
Russian Federation of June 1, 1992 No. 371 "On urgent energy saving measures in the field of
extraction, production, transportation and utilization of oil, gas and oil products" in view of providing
financial support to government energy saving policy.
2.    The Energy Saving Fund performs its activities on regional as well as on federal
levels as an entity comprised of regional extra-budgetary Energy Saving Funds, set up under
paragraph 1, article 20 of the Russian Federation Law of March 5, 1992 "On Soviets of People's
Deputies of krajs and oblasts and on Administrations of krajs and oblasts" as well as of the Federal
extra-budgetary fund.
3.    The Energy Saving Fund is managed, in conformity with Regulation of the Russian
Federation Government of June 1, 1992 No. 371, by the Management Board of the Energy Saving
Fund, incorporated within the system of the Ministry of Fuel and Energy of the Russian Federation as
an economically independent legal entity. The guidelines for the utilization of the Energy Saving
Fund's resources are coordinated by the Supervisory Board of the Fund, set up by the Ministry.
Coordination of works, connected with the utilization of resources of regional Energy Saving
Funds, is carried out by the relevant departments and energy committees of administrations of krajs and
oblasts. Regional Energy Saving Funds are managed by self-supporting energy saving centers and other
similar structures, set up by administrations of krajs and oblasts, on the accounts of which the resources
of those funds are accumulated.
The Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund provides methodical guidance and takes part
in the activities of energy saving centers of krajs and oblasts in view of promoting the government energy
saving policy and providing it with financial and technological support.
4. The Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund as well as the regional energy saving
centers are governed in their activities by the Constitution of the Russian Federation, laws and other
legal acts, adopted by congresses of people's deputies of the Russian Federation and by the Supreme
Soviet of the Russian Federation, decrees and ordinances of the President of the Russian Federation,
the Govermnent of the Russian Federation, charters of the Management Board of the Energy Saving
Fund and of regional energy saving centers, as well as by the present Regulation.



-61-
Annex 5-1
Page 8 of 12
Forming of the Energy Saving Fund
5. Resources of the Russian extra-budgetary Energy Saving Fund on federal and regional levels
are formed out of:
- quarterly remittances by industrial, construction and transport enterprises and organizations
of 0.5 per cent of cost of energy resources consumed over the previous quarter, including the
above sums into the costs of production (works, services) of the above enterprises;
- payments of statutory penal sanctions for irrational utilization of fuel and energy resources;
- resources allocated to the Management Board of the Fund and to regional energy saving
centers out of federal and local budgets as well as received from other sources for carrying
out their investment activities, financing research and development and other energy saving
programs, as well as those received in form of dividends, interests on deposits and on loans
and other proceeds from their economic and commercial activities;
- donations of legal and physical entities.
Administrations of krajs and oblasts are permitted to use part of resources of environmental funds to
form their regional Energy Saving Funds.
6. Industrial transport and construction enterprises, consuming fuel and energy resources, are
to make quarterly remittances, starting with the fourth quarter, 1992, to the accounts of federal and
regional funds, to the amount of 0.5 per cent of the cost of energy resources, actually consumed by
them in the previous quarter. The above sums are included into the cost of production (works,
services) of the above enterprises and organizations, as provided for by Regulation of the Government
of the Russian Federation of August 5, 1992 No. 552 "On adoption of the Regulation -n expenses on
production and sale, included in the costs of production (works, services) and on the methods of
accounting financial results for the purposes of taxation".
The above resources are to be remitted by enterprises and organizations to the Energy Saving
Fund on the following pro rata basis:
- to the Federal Energy Saving Fund - 30 per cent;
- to regional Energy Saving Funds at the place of location of enterprises and organizations 70 per
cent.
Budgetary organizations, providing services to population, public utilities and social sector
organizations, farms, collective farms, state farms are exempt from such payments.
Payments to Energy Saving Funds are effected for the consumption of the following fuel and
energy resources: natural gas, engine fuel, coal, electric and thermal energy. Administrative bodies of
krajs, oblasts, autonomies of the Russian Federation, of Moscow and St.Petersburg, as well as the
Ministry of Fuel and Energy of the Russian Federation advise the above enterprises and organizations
of account numbers of Energy Saving Funds.
7. In compliance with Regulation of the Russian Federation Government of June 1, 1992
No. 371 the Ministry of Fuel and Energy of the Russian Federation and the Ministry of Science,
Higher and Technical Education of the Russian Federation starting with 1993 provide joint financing
on an annual basis of research and development in the field of energy saving technologies out of
relevant extra-budgetary funds.
8. Payment of statutory penal sanctions for irrational utilization of fuel and energy
resources are earmarked for regional Energy Saving Funds on terms and conditions provided for by
law.



-62-
Annex 5-1
Page 9 of 12
9. The Ministry of Fuel and Energy of the Russian Federation as well as other ministries
and departments of the Russian Federation, which have set up extra-budgetary investment funds,
provide financing of energy saving expenditures, proposed by the Management Board of the Energy
saving - fund, connected with implementation of energy saving programs, to the amount of 1 per cent
of the relevant extra-budgetary investment funds.
10. Legal and physical entities, as a rule, make their donations to their local Energy Saving
Funds.
11. Enterprises and organizations, making remittances to the Federal as well as regional
Eenergy Saving Funds, charge the above sums to the debit of production accounts and to the credit
of account 67 of chart of accounts, approved by the Ministry of Economy and Finance of the Russian
Federation on December 19, 1991, No. 18-05.
Remittances are credited by banks to special accounts of Federal and regional Energy Saving
Funds, opened by the Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund as well as by energy saving
centers of krajs and oblasts and by other similar self-supporting organizations, set up by local
administrative bodies.
Responsibility for timely and accurate remittances of the above resources is borne by
enterprises and organizations, effecting such payments, as provided for by law.
Guidelines for utilization of the Energy Saving Fund's resources
12. Resources of the Federal fund are allocated for large-scale inter-industrial and inter-
regional energy saving programs mostly on the basis of equity participation in joint stock companies
and other enterprises and organizations, granting soft loans and other forms of activities.
Resources of the Federal fund are to be allocated mainly for the following purposes:
- financing of research and development, providing legal consultations and creating legal
framework as well as of working out energy consumption standards;
- supporting energy saving programs, taking into account their environmental aspects, on the
basis of equity participation with organizations, commissioning such works, the contribution of which
may not be less than 40 per cent of total costs of the above programs;
- financial support of the development of production of sophisticated energy saving equipment,
used for interindustrial purposes, of energy efficient energy utilizing equipment, produced on a
large-scale basis, as well as financing of creating the market infrastructure -or energy saving
equipment;
- financial support of energy saving projects, connected with the complex production of highly
efficient materials, constructions, equipment, technical re-tooling of enterprises, which ensures the
engagement of energy saving reserves;
- granting soft loans to small and medium-size energy saving projects, which have undergone
necessary technical and economic expert examination, directed at support of large-scale energy
saving programs, production of consumer goods, foodstuffs, development of farms, construction of
energy saving housing.
13. Resources of regional funds are allocated for financing of territorial interindustrial energy
saving programs, development of local base for the production of energy saving equipment used for
interindustrial purposes, financial support of activities as well as providing adequate technical
facilities to regional expert councils and extra-ministerial bodies, supervising the efficient utilization
of fuel and energy.



-63-
Annex 5-1
Page 10 of 12
14. Administrative bodies of krajs, oblasts, regional energy saving committees and other
relevant local organizations utilize resources of regional Energy Saving Funds as provided for by the
present Regulation.
15. Soft loans are granted by the Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund as well as
by energy saving centers of krajs and oblasts (and other similar structures) after approval of their
plans and programs by the Supervisory Board or by energy committees of krajs and oblasts
respectively. Interest payable on the above loans granted out of the Fund's resources is determined
by the Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund, as a rule, for two years to the amount of up to
50 per cent. Part of interest payments, not exceeding 20 per cent, is used for financing of the
Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund.
16. In view of providing additional economic incentives to enterprises, designing and
producing energy saving equipment, to ensure higher technical level of the above equipment and
increase its production volumes, according to decisions taken by energy committees as well as by the
Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund, resources of Federal and regional Energy Saving
Funds may be allocated for grants, given for the following purposes:
- equity participation in the financing of research and development in interindustrial fields up to 50 per cent
of its cost;
- development of production of trial lots of energy saving equipment, equipment for utilization of
non-traditional energy resources, equipment for utilization of local energy resources, recycled wastes and secondary
energy resources - up to 25 per cent;
- construction and refurbishing of buildings, ensuring higher energy efficiency, up to normative levels - 25 per
cent.
The amount of resources, allocated for grants given out of regional Energy Saving Funds, is not to exceed
20 per cent of their annual proceeds. Enterprises and organizations, consuming fuel and energy, which ensure
sustainable increase of utilization of secondary energy resources, in view of providing incentives to them, may be
refunded for 20 per cent of their expenditures, connected with the carrying out of such works.
Resources of the Energy Saving Fund are being used for setting up an information database of energy
efficient technologies, for creating a legal framework for the system of management, which is being established,
for insurance of investments into energy saving programs, made by domestic and foreign companies and firms,
for training of staff, including training abroad.
17.   Enterprises and organizations, ensuring the carrying out of expert examination of their energy
stocks to find reserves for energy saving with the help of special diagnostic devices, may be refunded for 25 per
cent of total costs of such works out of resources of Energy Saving Funds of krajs and oblasts.
Resources of the Energy Saving Fund are allocated primarily for providing incentives for examining energy
stock of enterprises and organizations consuming in excess of 30 thousand tones of coal equivalent, heating boilers
with the capacity in excess of 10 hectocalories per hour, heating units of magistral heating networks
18.   Benefits under pp. 15, 16 and 17 of the present Regulation are primarily granted to enterprises
and organizations, which ensure the carrying out of expert examinations of their energy stocks.
Supervision over forming and utilization
of resources of the Eneryv Saving Fund
19.   Enterprises and organizations, relevant government agencies, connected with the ensuring of



-64-
Annex 5-1
Page 11 of 12
proper utilization of the Energy Saving Fund resources on both federal and regional levels, are not permitted to
use the above resources for purposes, not provided for by the present Regulation.
20.   Supervision over the accuracy and timeliness of payments to federal and regional Energy
Saving Funds is to be carried out by the relevant administrative bodies of krajs and oblasts as well as by the
Energy Saving Fund Management Board.
21.   The Management Board of the Energy Saving Fund as well as self-supporting energy saving
centers set up by administrations of krajs and oblasts and other similar structures bear responsibility for the
proper utilization of the Fund's resources.
22.   Should the competent bodies detect the breach of the order of utilization of the Fund's
resources, provided for by the above Regulation, officials, directly responsible for the above wrongdoing/ are
to be brought to responsibility under the Russian Federation Rules of law in force. The Management Board of
the Russian Federation Extra-budgetary Inter-industrial Energy Saving Fund as well as regional energy saving
centers are to ensure the including of provisions on penal sanctions for breach of order of utilization of the
Fund's resources into contracts to be concluded with their employees.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
GAS DISTRIBUTION REHABILITATION AND ENERGY EFFICIENCY PROJECT
DESCRIPTION AND COSTS OF ENERGY CONSERVATION PROJECTS
1.    The objective of the Energy Efficiency component of the Gas Rehabilitation Project is to identify
and implement energy-saving measures that will increase the efficiency of natural gas use in the district
heating and combined heat and power plants and satisfy the criteria of economic and financial feasibility.
Natural gas use was assessed in four Russian cities - Ryazan, Saratov, Volgograd and Voronezh. All
these cities have similar gas supply systems.
2.    Typically all cities have two to five cogeneration plants owned by the energy utility; for example,
Ryazanenergo, which generates electricity and heat. The heating medium, usually hot water, is heated
in hot water boilers and/or steam-water heat exchangers by steam extracted from the turbines. The hot
water is pumped through the transmission piping to the intermediate substations owned by municipal
district heating companies that resell the heat to the customers. The power plants do not usually sell heat
directly to the customers, although there are exceptions. In turn, municipal companies usually have their
own hot water boilers, and besides the resold heat energy, sell hot water directly to the customers.
Generally, the customers do not have heat energy meters. The power plants and municipal boiler plants
have flow meters but often they are not accurate or not functioning. The nominal efficiency of boilers
installed at power plants is relatively high at close to 90 percent, although the real operational efficiency
is lower due to inadequate automatic combustion control, while that of the municipal boilers is very low,
ranging from 45 percent to 50 percent.
3.    The gas conservation measures recommended include:
-     burner and boiler replacements at municipal boilers;
-     implementation of portable control instrumentation sets for diagnostics at power plants
and municipal boilers to achieve better operational efficiency;
-     installation of automated control systems at boiler plants; and
-     installation of automatic temperature controls and heat energy meters at heat substations.
4.    During project preparation, a number of subprojects were identified for the DH/CHP plants in
each of the cities, all of which met the minimum EIRR requirement of 20 percent. During appraisal, a
selection of two to three subprojects in each city were advanced considerably and are ready for
preparation of draft tender documents. The full range of identified subprojects are summarized in Tables
1 through 4. Those projects whose preparation was advanced during the appraisal mission are described
in Section A to D below and summarized in Tables 5 through 8. All of the projects satisfy the criterion
of a 20-percent internal rate of return on gas savings except a proposed US$ 3 million boiler replacement
project in Voronezh where the return on gas savings remains at 14 percent, but with other benefits at 19
percent.
A. City of Ryazan
5.    District heating is supplied from three primary sources including Ryazanenergo that owns two
co-generation power plants (Novoryazanskaya TETS and Dyagilevskaya TETS), the Municipal District
Heating Company and Industrial Boiler Plants.
6.    The Novo-Ryazanskaya TETS CO, Ltd is a producer of electricity, steam, and hot water, and



-67-
Annex 6-1
Page 2 of 9
was privatized in 1994. The station is rated at 400 MW electric production and installed thermal capacity
of 2009 Gcal/hr. There is approximately 160 km of piping in this system. The district heating piping
is generally above ground until it reaches the city substation interfaces. There are approximately 100
substations in the system and each substation serves 10 to 20 buildings. Some older apartment buildings,
large factories, and hospitals typically have their own heating boilers. In 1993 the power plant consumed
about 1 million m3 of natural gas and some 300,000 tons of mazut. The power production was about
2,000 GWh.
7.    The gas saving recommendations include:
-     installation of automated controls and replacing the burners at boilers; and
-     implementation of a portable set of control instrumentation for
diagnostics and control.
8.    The Municipal District Heating Company (municipal department) has a heat-only capacity of
1,545 Gcal/hr. Recommended gas-saving measures include installation of automated temperature controls
and heat energy meters at 35 heat substations;
9.    The total implementation cost for the selected district heating gas conservation projects in Ryazan
is $ 2.0 million and the expected reduction in gas consumption is 22 million m3 per year.
B.    City of Saratov
10.   District heating in Saratov is supplied from Saratovenergo - an open joint stock company
(privatized 1993) - which owns four cogeneration power plants, GRES, TETS 1, TETS 2, TETS 5 and
a district heating boiler plant, and the Municipal District Heating Company which owns 52 boiler plants
and 33 substations. Additional plants owned by various Saratov entities include Industrial Boiler Plants
consisting of 196 boiler facilities and District Heating Boiler Plants consisting of 304 heat-only boiler
facilities.
11.   The Municipal District Heating Company owns 52 boiler plants and 33 substations.
Thirty-three substations separate the responsibility of the central cogeneration plant system from the
municipal district heating systems. In 1993, total gas consumption by 52 municipally owned heat-only
boiler plants was reported as approximately 30,000 (1000) m3. The average efficiency of boiler plants
is 50-52 percent. Total peak heat load of the 52 boiler plants is estimated at 55 Gcal/hr. Dates of
installation range from 1954 to 1990.
12.   Gas saving recommendations include:
-     replacement of old burners at municipal boilers with new high-efficiency burners and
associated controls; and
-     implementation of a portable set of express control instrumentation for diagnostics and
monitoring.
13.   Saratovenergo Ltd owns Saratov GRES, TETS 1, TETS 2 and TETS 5. Saratov GRES has a
total capacity of 55 MW. The date of installation ranges from 1941 to 1985. There are nine steam
boilers installed, rated from 45 to 95 tons/hr, and a total heating capacity of 276 Gcal/hr. The GRES
produced and supplied to the customers 1,353,204 Gcal of heat in 1992. TETS I has a total capacity of
25 MW and the total heating capacity of the plant is 276 Gcallhr. The TETS produced and supplied to
the customers 976,440 Gcal of heat in 1992. The total gas consumption in 1992 was 140,505 (1000) in3
TETS 2 has a total capacity of 315 MW and a total heating capacity of 1,090 Gcal/hr. It produced and



-68-
Annex 6-1
Page 3 of 9
supplied to the customers 4,127,286 Gcal of heat in 1992. The total gas consumption in 1992 was
702,987 (1000) m3. TETS 5 has a total capacity of 440 MW (electric) and 1240 Gcal/hr (heating)
including 700 Gcal/hr - heating capacity of turbines. The total gas consumption for 1992 was 565,168
(1000) m3 and a the total amount of heat supplied was 2,537,892 Gcal. The total fuel consumption of
Saratovenergo for 1993 was 286,000 (1000) m3 of gas and 997,000 ton mazut.
14.   Saratovenergo supplies heat in transmission lines to the substations. This piping ranges in
diameter from 325 to 1220 mm. Lower diameter piping is the responsibility of the municipal district
heating company. There are approximately 500 km of primary piping installed in Saratov of which 200
km are owned by Saratovenergo. The balance is owned by the municipal district heating company and
industrial sites. The 33 substations are owned by the municipal district heating company.
15.   The gas saving measures include:
Saratovenergo:
- portable set of diagnostic instrumentation; and
- TETS-5 burner replacement and associated automatic combustion controls.
Municipal district heating company:
- burner replacement and associated automatic controls; and
- automatic temperature controls at heat substations.
16.   The total implementation cost for selected district heating gas conservation projects in Saratov
is $ 4.1 million and the expected reduction in gas consumption is 47 million m3 per year.
C.    City of Volgograd
17.   District heating is supplied from three primary sources including:
-      WVolgogradenergo (open joint stock company) which owns three cogeneration power
plants - GRES, TETS-2 and TETS-3, and 3 district heating boiler plants;
*     Municipal District Heating Company (municipal departement) owns 470 boilers with
1506 Gcal/h capacity and 170 heat substations; and
-     Industrial Boiler Plants consisting of 189 boiler facilities.
18.   The fuel consumed by Volgogradenergo was 2,900,000 (1000 m3) of gas and 580,000 ton of
mazut in 1993. The fuel consumed by the municipal district heating company was 427,000 (1000 m3) of
gas.
19.   The gas saving measures proposed for the Volgogradenergo include:
-     replacement of old burners with new high-efficiency burners with associated controls at
TETS 2 and 3.
20.   The Municipal District Heating Company owns total 470 boilers and 170 heat substations.
21.   The proposed gas saving measures are:
-     implementation of a portable set of control instrumentation for diagnostics and control;
and
-     installation of automatic temperature controls at 170 heatsubstations.



-69-
Annex 6-1
Page 4 of 9
22.   The total implementation cost for selected district heating gas conservation projects in Volgograd
is $ 3.6 million and the expected reduction in gas consumption is 69 million m3 per year.
D.    City of Voronezh
23.   District heating is supplied from three primary sources, including Voronezhenergo, the Municipal
District Heating Company, and Industrial Boiler Plants consisting of 10 boiler facilities.
24.   Voronezhenergo (open joint stock company) owns two cogeneration power plants - TETS- I and
TETS-2, a District Heating Company which purchases heat from cogeneration plants and two small boiler
rooms containing 8 boilers. TETS-1 and TETS-2 produce about 25 percent of the total heat consumed
by the city. The plants supply this heat to the Voronezhenergo DH Company which produces an
additional 10-12 percent of heat and sells all the heat produced by Voronezhenergo (35-37 percent of the
total heat supply) to its customers. TETS 1 has a heating capacity of 1,550 Gcal/hr and an electric
capacity of 165 MW; Voroneshenergo's fuel consumption in 1993 was 1,960,000 (1000 m3) gas and
106,000 ton of mazut.
Gas saving recommendations include:
-     replacement of burners and installation of associated automated controls at existing boilers
at TETS-1; and
-     Implementing a portable set of instrumentation for diagnostics and control of the existing
equipment.
The Municipal District Heating Company is a part of the city administration; its 137 boilers supply
about 20 percent of the city's district heating needs.
25.   Gas saving measures include:
-     replacement of old boilers with new high-efficiency boilers with associated controls; and
-     automatic temperature controls for heat substations.
26.   The total implementation cost for selected district heating gas conservation projects in Voronezh
is $ 4.4 million and the expected reduction in gas consumption is 37 million m3 per year.
E.    Summary - Tables 9 and 10 summarize the potential and the evaluated projects in the four cities,
in terms of total capital cost and total annual gas savings. In order to mazimize the impact of the
proposed investment in energy savings subprojects, RESF has been encouraged to replicate the "evaluate"
projects shown in Table 10 in other cities (initially, St. Petersburg, followed by Vladimir, Samara, Nizhi-
Novgorod, Rostov-on-Don and Stavropol). This would result in total expected gas savings of about 0.5
BCM per year, based on disbursement of the full amount of the loan directed towards energy efficiency
(US$60 million) is disbursed.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
GDC TRAINING/TWINNING ARRANGEMENTS
Summary
As gas distribution companies (GDC) in Russia move from state-ownership and control into being
investor-owned joint stock companies, there is an urgent need for substantial enlargement of their
management, financial, marketing and to a more limited extent, technical skills. Each GDC would
benefit from a significant number of its senior employees being exposed, in depth, to practices in
commercially run gas companies. The scale of the requirement would best be met by "twinning"
arrangements between Russian and foreign gas utilities. The overall cost for each twinning arrangement
is estimated to cost about US$1.6m over a two-year period.
Background
The process of transforming local GDCs in Russia into joint stock companies was initiated by Presidential
Decree in December 1992 and is now well advanced. Final clearance of each individual organization's
proposals has been given for about 50 percent of all the utilities by August 1993 and the remainder are
expected to be approved by November. Allocation of shares follows shortly after approval with
employees of an enterprises owning either 40 percent or 51 percent, depending on the variant chosen.
The remaining shares will be held for the next 3 years by various municipal and state bodies, after which
a decision will be made on further disposals.
Or2anization
The Board of Directors of the new companies will initially comprise the General Director of the
company, representatives of municipal and state bodies and a Trade Union representative. Internally,
companies will probably follow existing practice, using a Management Board comprising the General
Director, Deputy Directors and up to 5-10 Heads of Departments. Employee numbers range from a few
hundred in the smaller city companies up to several thousand in the larger city and regional companies.
Typically, they are organized into 3 main functional groups - engineering (employing the great majority),
economic and financial (mainly billing and bookkeeping) and general administrative services (stores and
buildings).
Training Requirements
Six gas distribution companies (Ryazan, Voronezh, Volgograd, Saratov, Stavropol and Rostov-on-the
Don) have been visited by World Bank missions to prepare the proposed Gas Distribution Reconstruction
and Energy Efficiency Project. These missions have concluded that the senior management of the
companies need to upgrade their skills to enable them to manage their new business entities in a
commercial environment.  They appear to be proceeding on the assumption that their present
organizational structure will continue to be appropriate and that management issues and practices will be
little changed. This is understandable in the light of the many other urgent problems that they have had
to handle over the last year, but does not provide a good basis for taking advantage of their new status.
Managers in the GDCs will in fact need to adapt rapidly to a changing environment, and to take
responsibility for their business strategies and decisions. In these new circumstances, there are many



-76-
Annex 6-2
Page 2 of 24
areas where existing staff have not had the exposure to the business skills which they will require. This
is not to doubt that the staff have the capacity to acquire these skills but, up to now, there have been no
means for them to do so.
Senior managers in all the utilities visited regret that they have had little or no opportunity for contacts
with their counterparts in gas utilities operating abroad. They are particularly concerned about this in
respect of engineering developments and are less focused on the gap in management practices because
of the lack of familiarity with business management in a market economy. However, there is a general
awareness of the need to develop new skills and there is no doubt that initiatives to meet this need would
be welcomed.
Such institutional reform and development would require staff at a range of different levels to be trained
to understand more fully the demands placed on an investor-owned company in a market economy, and
to acquire the professional expertise that is necessary to meet these demands. The scope for training is,
therefore, considerable, both in the range of topics to be covered and in the number of managers within
each company who would benefit.
In view of the urgency of initiating comprehensive training arrangements, it is desirable to proceed
simultaneously on a number of fronts. For example, training should be provided in parallel both for the
top management and for middle-management. A good grounding in business management practices for
top managers will pay off quickly only if trained supporting staff are available to implement the new
procedures. Conversely, training middle-management specialists in modern techniques could lead to
disillusion and cynicism if top management is then not receptive and eager to use these techniques in its
decision-making.
The specialist areas that should be covered include business planning, accounting and finance, investment
appraisal, systems development (including use of computers), sales and marketing, procurement,
personnel management, shareholder services and efficiency improvement. Some technical training may
be provided by consultants and contractors as part of reconstruction and energy efficiency projects but
more comprehensive training in modern gas distribution engineering practices is also desirable. These
areas are set out in more detail in the Annex.
For the most senior management in each company more general training should be provided on managing
a joint stock company, including accountability to a Board of Directors and shareholders. Senior
managers also require a broad initiation into the more specialist areas so that they can be aware of their
potential benefits and properly manage the newly-trained staff.
Length and Character of Training
Management and technical skills cannot be acquired overnight and theoretical classroom training is of
limited value. To meet the objectives outlined above, a significant number of employees in each GDC
should spend a period of at least three months, and preferably six months, working in a gas distribution
utility abroad. Experience has shown (particularly the technical assistance component of the Marshall
Plan) that on-the-job training of this kind is effective in communicating both theoretical and practical
knowledge, while stimulating enthusiasm and a desire to exercise the new skills. It is important,
however, to recognize that the trainee must have acquired a working knowledge of the language of the
host country before embarking on the training process. It would be impractical to provide an interpreter
with each trainee and much of value would be lost if language were a serious obstacle. Trainees should,
therefore, receive adequate language training before travel and the cost should be included in the financial



-77-
Annex 6-2
Page 3 of 24
provision. The rapid acquisition of language skill also requires enthusiasm and commitment and this
suggests that more trainees should probably be drawn from younger rather than older age groups; this
would, in any cases, be desirable so that this program produces a cadre of trained and motivated middle-
management for the future.
For the most senior management in each company, it is unlikely that personnel could be absent for an
extended period or that they would be able quickly to acquire a new language skill. Training for this
group, therefore, should take the form of shorter courses and seminars covering general management at
which interpreters would be available.
Organization of Training
The desirable emphasis of on-the-job training, coupled with the significant number of staff to be included
from each GDC, strongly suggests that such training would most effectively be organized within a
"twinning" arrangement. A Russian GDC would be linked with a similar company in North America
or Western Europe on a continuing basis, providing benefits over a long period. In the initial two years,
some 4-6 individuals (depending on the size of the GDC) would spend six months with the host company,
the arrangement to be repeated four times. The advantage of this approach is that it would not overly
tax the host GDC and would provide an opportunity to reinforce new skills as each team returns (there
is a danger that the skills will be lost if periodic reinforcement isn't established).
The Russian and the host companies would each appoint a co-ordinating officer who would set up the
necessary arrangements, monitor the progress of the trainees and develop the program in the light of
experience. The program should be intensive, with readily identifiable deliverables for each team.
Towards the end of the initial two-year period, the further training needs of the Russian GDC would be
jointly reviewed and a continuing program developed.
A preliminary estimate of the cost of such a program suggests, that the direct cost of sending an
individual to a foreign utility for a six-month period could be US$25,000 with a similar sum incurred
by the host utility in providing the facilities. For a group of six individuals repeated four times over a
two-year, the total would be US$1 .2m per company. In addition, the arrangements for short-term visits
and seminars for training senior management would cost a further US$0.4m.
Conclusion
A twinning arrangement between a Russian GDC and a foreign utility would provide an effective means
of organizing urgently needed business, professional and technical training for middle and senior
management. The business and personal contacts made should also prove valuable to both Russian and
foreign utilities over the longer-term.



-78-
Annex 6-2
Page 4 of 24
OUTLINE OF TRAINING PROGRAM ELEMENTS
FOR RUSSIAN GAS DISTRIBUTION COMPANIES
1.    General business training for senior managers, covering the main principles of the topics covered
in the program, plus a top-down view of business management, Board responsibilities and the role of a
Chief Executive.
2.    Financial control, including medium term forecasting, budgetary control, regular financial
reporting to management, internal audit, external audit, working capital control - cash, debtors and stock.
3.    Accounting, including balance sheets and profit and loss accounts, funds statements, accounting
principles, accounts preparation by computer, including software packages, accounts interpretation,
according to internationally accepted standards.
4.    Investment appraisal, including capital and revenue projects, cost estimation, discounted cash flow
and payback calculations, project reporting procedures, and post project appraisal.
5.    Personnel policies, including terms of employment, recruitment, training, termination procedures,
appraisal, motivation and incentive options.
6.    Information technology, including capital and revenue projects, cost estimation, discounted cash
flow and payback calculations, project reporting procedures, and post project appraisal.
7.    Gas market development, including consumer relations, load building, close involvement with
decision makers for energy choice, technical advice on energy efficiency, gas burning appliance
development, cost reduction for gas installation, identifying and meeting gaps in the energy market.
8.    Engineering techniques such as network design, leakage detection, use of plastic pipe, etc.
9.    Environmental monitoring and assessment.
10.   Overall balancing of demand and supplying with a view to optimizing costs/revenues and hence
profit, development of flexible sales contracts, tariff design to achieve optimum results, evaluation of
options for local supply side investments.
11.   Regulatory relations (on the basis that a regulatory framework will be put in place in due course),
including the purpose, costs and benefits, dealing with the regulator and possible other authorities
involved in the process.
12.   Efficiency improvement, using techniques such as operations research, organization and methods,
productivity analysis.
13. An overview of all the elements, bringing together their essential features and inter-relationships.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
RUSSIA ENERGY SAVINGS FUND - INSTITUTIONAL SUPPORT
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
BACKGROUND
The Russia Gas Distribution Rehabilitation and Energy Efficiency Project includes a component for
financing energy efficiency investments by local district heating and combined-heat-and-power (CHP)
utilities. It is proposed that the Russian Energy Savings Fund act as an overseer and administrator for
the Government of Russia, assisting in the identification and appraisal of sub-projects, advising companies
on implementation issues, and monitoring the financial viability and loan repayment aspects of the
subsidiary loans.
OBJECTIVES & SCOPE OF WORK
In order to successfully fulfil this proposed role, the RESF requests the assistance of consultants who will
provide technical support and training during the initial period of operation. Specifically, short-term
experts are required during the first year of the project implementation for one month per quarter (four
months/year) in each of the following skill areas:
-energy engineering
-project economics/financial analysis
-accounting/administration
The scope of work for these experts will be to assist and advise the counterpart RESF personnel whose
skill requirements and responsibilities are described in the attached statement of resource requirements.
The consultant would also be required to provide an additional technical specialist in energy engineering
for three weeks every quarter in year one, and for three weeks twice a year during years two and three.
The qualifications of this technical advisor would be defined by the RESF according to the specific, and
as yet unknown, sectoral and/or technology-specific needs of the project. RESF anticipates that different
specialists would be required for most or all of these advisory visits. It is also expected that the advisors
would serve only the staff of the RESF in year one; in subsequent years, they would advise both the
RESF and the technical staff of municipal companies, with RESF acting as coordinator for the training
missions.
Finally, the consultant would be required to design and deliver two five-day seminars once or twice a
year, focussed on special topics of relevance to the project, as defined by the RESF. These requirements
will only be defined after the Project Implementation Unit is staffed and has operated for at least 3
months.



-80-
Annex 6-2
Page 6 of 24
RESF Resource Requirements
Based on the proposed role of the RESF, and a review of its current staffing, capabilities and
organization, resource requirements are identified as follows:
Senior Management: Whatever form the RESF implementation group takes, it will need a senior
manager capable of providing leadership to the group by (i) setting out strategies and policies for the
group of technical specialists; (ii) providing operating plans and directions for the group and coordinating
and monitoring its activities; and (iii) serving as liaison with senior levels of both local and federal
government organizations and foreign (multilateral and bilateral) agencies.
Energy Engineering Expertise: An energy expert is required to satisfy the technical aspects of RESF'S
mandate. The energy technology expert would carry out the technical review and assessment of the sub-
projects, participate in the technical aspects of procurement, tendering and project management and
control; in conjunction with the engineering specialist, lead the evaluation of completed subprojects and
the project as a whole; and, assist in the identification and appraisal of additional energy efficiency
projects. Ideally, the individual would possess university level training in engineering and experience
with relevant energy efficiency technologies and projects in the industrial and buildings sectors. He
should be familiar with methods of energy auditing, although it is expected that much of this type of work
would be contracted out by the RESF. Communications skills are important as the engineer would need
to liaise with counterpart staff in the municipal utilities.
Economic and Financial Analysis Expertise: The RESF project economist/financial analyst would be
responsible for the economic and financial aspects of the review and assessment of subprojects; the
preparation of loan agreements with municipal utilities; assisting in the design and implementation of
project management and control systems; and participating in subproject and project evaluation activities.
The analyst would also be responsible for liaison with counterpart staff in Russian government agencies,
international organizations and the municipal utilities.
Accounting/Administration: The accountant/administrator would be charged with carrying out the
project accounting functions of the RESF group, as well as administrative tasks related mainly but not
entirely to project on-lending and implementation activities. The accountant should have skills in both
financial and management accounting.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
ENERGY PRICING STRATEGY STUDY
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
INTRODUCTION
1.    The World Bank is providing assistance to the Russian Federation in its efforts to commercialize
the energy supply sector. As part of this process, the Ministry of Fuels and Energy has requested that
the Bank finance a study to assist them in developing a strategy for making the transition from controlled
to market-defined pricing of energy products.
OBJECTIVES
2.    The objectives of the study are three-fold: to assist the Government in defining realistic long-term
objectives in terms of introducing competitive price setting in the energy sub-sectors; to assist them in
identifying interim measures and initiatives required to ensure an orderly transition from the current to
the targeted pricing regime; and to outline a program and time-frame for achieving the shift to market-
based pricing.
BACKGROUND
3.    The Government has made rapid progress in corporatizing the Russian energy sector. Many of
the primary and secondary supply enterprises have already been converted to joint stock companies, and
plans are underway to continue this process. At the same time, concerted efforts have been made to
adjust and index energy prices, through a series of degrees, in order to bring them closer to world prices
and to reflect the competitive positions of alternative energy forms. Ultimately, it is the goal of the
Government to extract itself, insofar as possible, from the pricing process, and allow market forces to
define energy prices in the country.
4.     This process of shifting from controlled prices to pricing based on market forces raises a number
of concerns, both in the long term and in the short term, regarding the strategies which the government
should adopt to ensure that a system of fair and competitive pricing is achieved. In the longer term,
concerns center on the steps required to ensure a competitive framework for energy supply both within
and among energy sectors. This includes defining realistic objectives with regard to establishing
competition among suppliers, both in terms of market entry and access to customers, identifying strategies
to facilitate the development of competitive markets both for individual fuels and among alternative forms
of energy, and defining a regulatory process to be implemented in those sectors where competition may
be difficult or impossible to achieve in an efficient manner.
5.    In the near term, concerns center around the strategies to be adopted during the period of
transition from controlled to market prices. These include identifying mechanisms to adjust for
distortions in the cost bases of suppliers which could inhibit fair competition, developing a fair and
equitable basis for reflecting differences in the costs of supply to different markets, making provision to
protect the basic needs of vulnerable groups, ensuring that "lifeline" supplies can be obtained at
affordable cost, and defining a schedule and milestones for the transition from regulated to competitive
pricing.



-82-
Annex 6-2
Page 8 of 24
SCOPE OF WORK
6.    The proposed Energy Pricing Strategy Study is intended to provide guidance to government in
defining appropriate strategies and policies relating to the establishment of a competitive framework for
energy pricing. The study will address both long term issues related to sector and sub-sector objectives,
and transitional strategies to be adopted in moving from current to targeted pricing mechanisms.
7.    In meeting these objectives, it is anticipated that the consultant would carry out the following
tasks:
Phas I
i.    Review the structure of the commercial energy supply industry in Russia, including petroleum
products, coal, natural gas, LPG, district heat and electricity, and the degree of competition
currently prevailing among alternative sources of supply. Where necessary, both wholesale and
retail supply systems should be considered. In addition, where transformation is involved
(thermal electric generation, district heating, petroleum refining), issues relating to competition
among alternative primary energy sources should be reviewed.
ii.    Review and identify any barriers to long-term competition within and among sectors, including
supply constraints, restricted access to markets, and financial and institutional barriers to entry.
iii.    Define a set of realistic long-term objectives for each energy sub-sector in terms of the degree
to which market mechanisms should be used to define energy prices, and identify the steps which
need to be taken to achieve this level of competition.
Pha. e II
iv.    Where barriers are deemed prohibitive, review alternative mechanisms for regulatory price
control, and provide detailed recommendations on the preferred regulatory process. These
recommendations should include the body or bodies responsible for regulation, the basis on which
regulated prices should be set (including appropriate parameters with regard to rate base, rate of
return, etc), and the suggested means of ensuring efficient performance in the absence of open
competition (proficiency audits, price caps, etc).
V.     Review and outline current sources of distortion in the cost structures of energy supply
comnpanies, which could lead to inefficient short term pricing decisions. Many of the newly
privatized enterprises will, for example, have acquired assets at well below net replacement value.
Hence, pricing based on historic cost could lead to fuel choices which are, in the long term, less
than desirable. Conversely, pricing at marginal cost, while sending more correct pricing signals
to markets, would create returns to shareholders which would likely be incompatible with the
level of investment.
vi.    Review and evaluate mechanisms that could be used to adjust for short-term cost distortions (for
example, production taxes, sales taxes, corporatization loans, dividends on government equity).
Recommend preferred alternatives and provide a schedule for both implementing and for phasing
out adjustinent mechanisms as distortions are eliminated. Evaluation of alternatives should take
into account the longer-term needs of the private corporations to raise funds for expansion and
to provide a reasonable return on invested capital.



-83-
Annex 62
Page 9 of 24
vii.   Review and make recommendations on the need for reduced rates on "lifeline" energy supplies.
The review should address the level of basic energy needs, as well as the degree of subsidy
required to ensure affordable lifeline supply. The study should also address and make specific
recommendations on the preferred method for financing lifeline subsidies (transfer of general tax
revenues, special taxes, cross-subsidization, etc.).
viii.  In subsectors where regulated prices are proposed as the preferred pricing mechanism, review
and make recommendations on the manner in which the Government should deal with the
disaggregation of prices by region. Specific recommendations on spatial disaggregation of gas
prices should be addressed.
ix.    Provide a program for transition from the current regulated pricing mechanisms to the proposed
long-term market based pricing structure. The program should identify steps and initiatives to
be undertaken by the government in order to achieve long-term pricing objectives, and should
indicate milestones at which these government actions should occur.
x.    Provide a schedule for transition, taking into account the past and proposed rate of change in the
energy industry, and the time that might be required in order to ensure an orderly progress
towards competitive pricing.
DELIVERABLES
8.    An interim report is required at the end of Phase I (task iii), summarizing the findings with regard
to the current structure of the energy supply industry, the barriers to competition within the industry, and
the recommended long-term objectives which should be pursued in terms of competitive energy pricing.
Provided that these objectives have been established in a satisfactory manner, the consultant will carry
out the remaining study tasks, and provide a draft final report covering findings and recommended
strategies and schedules for transition.
9.    Subsequent to review, the consultant will make agreed amendments and provide a final report,
integrating both phases of the study.



-84-
Annex 6-2
Page 10 of 24
RUSSIAN FEDERATION
THIRD PARTY ACCESS STUDY
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
INTRODUCTION
1.    The Ministry of Fuels and Energy of the Russian Federation has requested financing from the
World Bank to establish a Third Party Access Regulation (TPAR) for gas transit in Russia. The
Government's objective in establishing the TPAR is to achieve increased economic efficiency in the
delivery of energy. Institutions and incentives should be established to ensure that access to gas delivery
and pricing mechanisms would protect the interests of the consumer and establish incentives for investors
in the gas sector to achieve reasonable returns on their investments as a result of efficient operation.
Furthermore, the Government is interested in minimizing the administrative burden of regulation by
maximizing price clearing mechanisms through competition.
BACKGROUND
2.    Russia's gas markets continue to evolve rapidly.  Western and Central European markets
stagnated during the period from 1990 to 1992, as a result of slow economic growth in the west and
restructuring in central Europe. These markets have shown some rebound during 1993 and have
accelerated in early 1994. However, increases in these gas markets have been offset by the decline in
Russia's gas delivery to the Republics of the Former Soviet Union. Gas prices to these markets have
been increased considerably, resulting in a market contraction and a build-up of accounts receivable. In
many of these markets the accounts receivable issue are substantial, and an as yet unresolved problem.
3.    As both domestic and export markets continue to adjust, energy supply and interfuel substitution
is expected to change to reflect the evolving market. Given Russia's enormous gas reserves, its relatively
low cost of supply and its relatively low impact on the environment, gas is expected to continue to
dominate as the primary fuel of choice for Russia. However, as energy markets in Russia contract with
decreasing economic activity, industrial restructuring and energy conservation investments, gas supply
is expected to become market constrained. As a result, gas is expected to saturate the primary energy
supply market in Russia over the medium term.
4.    In markets where gas becomes demand constrained, gas pricing is expected to reflect gas-on-gas
competition (assuming barriers to market access are eliminated and multiple suppliers of gas supply can
compete for the available markets). In Russia, Gazprom currently enjoys an exclusive monopoly on non-
associated gas production and gas transmission. This study is designed to assist the Government in
implementing policies that would make the evolving gas markets more efficient by increasing competition
as a market clearing mechanism, while preserving the rights and obligations of the consumers and
suppliers.
SCOPE OF WORK
5.    The consultant' would draft a Third Party Access Regulation to enable the Government to
establish a mechanism to enable increased economic efficiency of energy delivery. The consultant would,
I/The term 'consultant" is meant to reflect a group of experts whose skill rnix would enable them to provide a
wide ranging set of advice to the Government regarding Third Party Access Regulation.



-85-
Annex 6-2
Page 11 of 24
based on his understanding of the Russian energy system and knowledge of Third party Access practices
implemented elsewhere, assist the Ministry of Fuels and Energy in drafting this regulation. The tasks
to be undertaken by the consultant would include, but not be limited to the following:
6.    Task 1: The consultant would supplement his understanding of the energy delivery system in
Russia through interviews with the officials from the Ministry of Fuels and Energy, Gazprom, Oil
Producing Associations (producing associated gas), Rossgazifikazia, newly formed gas distribution
companies and major gas consumers. Through this exercise, the consultant would assess the impediments
to the development of gas-on-gas competition in Russia and suggest ways in which these barriers might
be removed to bring about a more competitive gas industry.
7.    Task 2: The consultant would outline the main problems associated with establishing Third Part
Access in Russia. The consultant would prepare a written report which, drawing on experience in other
countries, sets out the main options that the Government could use to address these issues and how they
could be implemented as laws, regulations and/or decrees affecting the transport of gas. Unbundling of
other services (such as storage) would also be reviewed and specific recommendations made.
8.    Task 3: After a review of the options with the Government and the Bank, the consultant would
collaborate with officials in the Ministry of Fuels and Energy in drafting specific decrees, laws and/or
regulations on Third Party Access and related matters.

Annex 6-2
