RUSSIAN FEDERATION
GAS DISTRIBUTION REHABILITATION AND
ENERGY EFFICIENCY PROJECT
FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
I.    This Annex outlines the assumptions used in the financial analysis of the project, and details the
findings of the assessment of the financial internal rates of return.
A.    Financial Rate of Return - Metering
2.     Gas distribution companies would benefit financially from the meter installation program only
through more accurate measurement of quantities purchased and sold. More accurate measurement has
two potential impacts: first, it will eliminate the under-reading of industrial sales and purchases that is
currently experienced as a result of the age of the meters; and secondly, it will help to reduce or
eliminate the "losses" now incurred as a result of billing by norms. (The GDCs purchases from Gazprom
are metered, but sales to residential and un-metered commercial customers are estimated based on the
norms. The sum of the quantities purchased and quantities billed do not always match.)
3.    In estimating the benefits of metering, it was assumed that meters would eliminate the losses now
incurred as a result of billing commercial customers based on norms. It was also assumed that, once
the meters were installed, commercial customers would reduce their consumption to the level of the
norms. Hence commercial sales volumes would remain unchanged (except for projected changes in the
demand forecast), but the gas purchased to supply these needs would decline by the level of estimated
"losses". Current commercial losses as a percent of commercial sales were estimated by the consultants
as being 0. 1 percent for Volgograd Gorgas.
4.    In the industrial sector, financial benefits were based on the premise that existing meters are
under-reading both sales and purchases by 1 to 2 percent. The benefits calculation assumed that more
accurate metering would result in an increase in sales and purchases equal to 1 percent of current and
projected industrial sales.
5.     For residential customers, it was originally anticipated that metering would yield benefits similar
to those in the commercial sector - although to a much greater degree since norms were judged to be
significantly less than actual consumption. However, the norms for residential gas usage have recently
been increased to as much as double their previous levels. This increase should effectively eliminate most
discrepancies between the amounts billed and the amounts consumed. Consequently, the installation of
residential meters was assumed to yield no financial benefit to the utilities.
6.     Other assumptions relating to the calculation of FIRR on the metering component of the projects
are as follows:
Retail gas prices (constant 1993 price levels, excluding VAT):
Commercial, industrial                           US$40 per MCM by December, 1995
Residential                                      US$40 per MCM by December, 1997
Retail margins (% of purchase price):
Commercial, industrial                                    13.9%



-131-
Annex 8-1
Page 2 of 6
Residential                                            455.6%
Capital Cost of Projects:
As per cost estimate (expressed in 1993 price levels) plus 5 percent duties on foreign currency
components. Grant financing under the Global Environment Facility was deducted from the
capital costs to determine FIRRs of the implementing agencies.
Installation of Meters:
In accordance with cost estimates for each utility
7.    The table on pages 4 shows the calculation of FIRR on the metering component of the project
for Volgograd Gorgas. The estimated FIRR is 12.3 percent.
B     Financial Rate of Return - Metering with Rental Charge
8.    Clearly, the metering component of the project is not financially viable from the perspective of
the Gorgas under the base assumptions with regard to prices. While the potential economic benefits are
substantial in terms of induced conservation and consequent freeing up of gas for export, financial
returns to the gas distribution company are minimal and insufficient to support the investment required.
Possible approaches to resolving this problem include:  (a) recovering part and/or all of the meters'
costs through a demand charge and/or through direct customer contributions; or (ii) setting gas prices
based on a defined return on assets.
9.     Under the first alternative, customer charges or contributions would have to cover a substantial
proportion of meter capital costs. Assuming that on-lending to the gas distribution companies is at a
nominal rate of 10 percent, repayable in foreign currency, financial returns on the project would have
to be in the order of 7.5 percent in real terms simply to cover borrowing costs. Allowing for a return
on equity of 15 percent, and assuming a long-term capital structure of 60:40 debt/equity, a minimum real
FIRR requirement would be in the order of 10 percent. To achieve a 10 percent FIRR on the metering
component of the project, Volgograd Gorgas would have to recover 92 percent of the capital costs of the
meters from their customers. Given that the unit costs for meters are estimated to be US$90 for private
homes, US$840 for conmmercial customers, US$500 for apartment blocks (which would serve
approximately 10 customers each), and US$17,000 for industrial meters, an attempt to finance the
meters through up-front customer contributions is constrained by customer ability to pay.
10.   Alternatively, the Gorgas could impose a demand charge which would recover the financial cost
of the meters (including a 10 percent return on investment) over a more extended period. Recovering
the full cost of the meters over 10 years would require monthly demand charges as shown in the table
below. If the Gorgas were to recover only that percentage of meter cost required to yield an overall 10
percent return on the investment (i.e. 92 percent on average), demand charges would be reduced
accordingly.



-13 2-
Annex 8-1
Page 3 of 6
Annually                    Monthly
Commercial                                       $138.00                      $11.40
Private Homes                                     $14.65                       $1.20
Apartment Blocks (per unit)                        $8.15                       $0.70
Industrial                                      $2,767.00                    $230.60
11.   To place these demand charges in perspective, residential norms for cooking plus water heating
(typical of an apartment unit) are in the order of 85 m3 per month which, at US$40.00 per MCM would
suggest an energy charge of US$3.40. The demand charge would therefore represent roughly a 20
percent increase in the monthly bill. For private homes, which are likely to also use gas for heating,
norms would be in the order of 600 m3 per month, giving a projected monthly energy charge of
US$24.00. The demand charge of US$1.20 represents only a 5 percent increase in the monthly bill.
The charges are also moderate relative to incomes, as average monthly incomes are approaching
US$200, and total household incomes are therefore presumably approaching an average in the order of
US$300-400/month.
12.   Assuming that the meter rental charges are set to fully recover the capital costs of meters and
meter installation (assuming a capital recovery requirement of 10 percent/10 years), the FIRR on the
meter component of the project would rise to 14.5 percent. The table on page 5 provides details of these
calculations.
C.    Financial Rate of Return - Network Improvements
13.   Financial returns to Volgograd Gorgas as a result of investments in network improvements
(cathodic protection, SCADA, network analysis tools) will accrue from the same sources as the economic
returns; that is, a combination of deferred pipe replacement, reductions in investment requirements, and
reductions in electricity costs.
14.   For the financial analysis, the cost of pipe replacement was estimated to be US$114 per meter.
This presumes that Russian prices for materials would be equal to international prices, but that prices for
local services and labor would be only 40 percent of international prices.
C.,
15.   Annual savings in pipe replacement were calculated as the difference between replacing the full
network over a 20 year period and replacing it over 60 years. In addition, the use of more sophisticated
network analysis tools was assumed to add an additional benefit equal to 10 percent of this saving.
Annual savings in electricity costs were based on consultant estimates of savings in kWh consumed, and
an electricity cost of US$65.00 per MW.h.
16.   Page 6 summarizes the assumptions of the financial analysis for the network improvement
components of the project, and shows the estimated FIRR. Financial returns on this component are
estimated at 34.9 percent.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
GAS DISTRIBUTION REHABILITATION AND
ENERGY EFFICIENCY PROJECT
RISK ANALYSIS AND MANAGEMENT
Background
1.    Projects in Russia, in general, face a considerable degree of risk. Many of the risks are
attributable to the uncertainties associated with the stability of the macroeconomic environment. In
addition, project specific risks include uncertainties on equipment and installation costs and the
effectiveness of the equipment. As the newly formed joint stock companies have acquired new
responsibilities and reporting requirements (to their shareholders), some initial problems will arise
affecting their ability to perform effectively.
2.     As a result of the potential impacts of these uncertainties, a risk analysis was undertaken with
a goal of managing these risks to avoid unacceptable project returns. The process of determining a
risk mitigation strategy consisted of the following steps:
(1)    establish a risk list based on a brain-storming session with technical, economic, and financial
specialists experienced in similar projects;
(2)    establish a risk mitigation list of possible remedies that would address the risks identified in
(1);
(3)    model the stochastic nature of project implementation without the risk mitigation to determine
the expected rate of return and its probability distribution;
(4)    analyze the impact of each of the risk mitigation strategies to determine the impact on
expected returns and its variance;
(5)    formulate a risk mitigation strategy based on an analysis of the costs and expected benefits of
the proposed risk actions.
Risks
-Rik List .. .     3.      The risk list compiled identified a set of key risks indicated in
Table 1. The primary risks are associated with the cost of the
Equipment Quantity  equipment and the expected value of the benefits. The equipment costs
Equipment UnitCt        are subject to a number of uncertainties.  The most likely costs assume
Equiipment Unit Costs   standard foreign costs based on the assumption that Russian costs will
Output Prices        approach foreign costs (or greater) as input subsidies are reduced,
Energy Savings       based on experience in Central Europe. However, Russian supplied
Investment Plan       equipment could remain low if manufacturers take advantage of low
Russian labor costs while upgrading equipment quality to meet
international standards. There is a possibility this scenario could take
place as a number of foreign manufacturers have either established joint ventures or they are actively
pursuing this option. Equipment costs could also decrease if equipment is supplied from another
country whose labor costs are low, as has been the experience on other similar projects. Installation
costs could also be less than that assumed if the Gas Distribution Companies (GDCs) increase
productivity. This may happen as a result of their becoming joint stock companies and based on
guidance from their foreign partner. Some productivity gains could be initiated by reducing the size
of the teams responsible for construction. The quantity of equipment required could also vary



-137-
Annex 8-2
Page 2 of 5
depending on the evolution of the gas market. Some companies could go bankrupt, and no longer
need the equipment. However, new companies may require gas supply as investments take place.
Migration would also have an affect on the size of the market and, hence, the equipment needs.
4.    The project benefits are a function of a number of assumptions that are fraught with
uncertainty. The pace of the price reform program has an affect on the value of the benefits and
market size (as energy efficiency responses take place). The size of the market is a function of many
assumptions on the reform program, including economic growth and the structure of the economy.
Not only is there uncertainty with respect to the nature of the reforms, but also the rate at which they
are implemented.
5.     The size of the benefits associated with energy savings depends on the effectiveness of the
equipment, systems design and equipment maintenance. The "most likely" case represents a
conservative estimate of the benefits, to compensate for potential implementation problems and poor
maintenance. However, as companies are privatized, the incentives to succeed will mitigate this
problem.
6.    The benefits of the asset life extension component are also uncertain. The consultant
responsible for project preparation assessed the benefits from this program to represent 10 percent of
the investment program costs based on experience abroad. However, based on the system design in
Russia, the consultant estimated that these benefits could be as high as 15 percent of the investment
program. In addition to this fundamental assessment of the potential benefits of asset life extension
are the uncertainties inherent in the investment program itself, including the amount of equipment
required and the unit costs of this equipment.
Risk Mitigation Actions                                              Risk MIitigiation List
7.     Given that the project is subject to many uncertainties, project  Pr* Refo
design and proposed implementation were formulated to reduce the risk   nrice Reform
of achieving unsatisfactory financial returns. Given that the economic  Institutonal Reform
returns are robust, and that the GDCs have become joint stock         Training/Twinning
companies with 51 percent private ownership, it was determined that   Phased Procurement
the analysis should focus on financial returns.                       Financial Covenants
Gas Market Analysis
8.     Most of the managers of the GDCs visited expressed concern     Procurerent Review
about the financial viability of the investments given the uncertainties
on price reform. The project design requires prices to industrial
customers (all customers except households) to reach US$40/MCM by
the end of 19951, when investments are expected to start to be commissioned. This risk has been
addressed by the following approach. Project processing has been contingent upon continued price
reform. The price increases during 1993, particularly the decree in July 1993 which increased
industrial prices and linked monthly adjustments to inflation, made project processing feasible as the
Government made its commitment to price reform clear. Continued process of gas prices has been a
condition of Board presentation.
1 The project financial benefits are not directly a function of price as the GDCs revenues are a function of
the differential between the purchase price from Gazprom and the selling price to the customer. However, the
Government has linked wholesale and retail price increases. Therefore, the margin associated with a
US$40/MCM of about US$5.00/MCM is the price objective for the GDCs.



-138-
Annex 8-2
Page 3 of 5
9.     Further measures in continued price reform are dealt with through the financial covenants.
The design of the covenants are based on three principles: (a) adequate internal cash generation to
fully finance local costs; (b) adequate internal cash generation to maintain a debt service ratio of 1.5;
and, (c) adequate returns on net revalued fixed assets (10 percent) to attract equity financing. These
covenants will be reviewed frequently during the project implementation cycle to ensure that project
financial viability is maintained. Because of the divisible nature of the project, procurement will be
closely linked to financial returns. A major, mid-term project review will take place to assess the
costs and benefits to ensure the continued viability of the project. Semi-annual reviews will be
undertaken to ensure that key indicators are being maintained such as, price levels, equipment costs,
installation costs, energy savings and overall network investment costs.
10.   Institutional reform is considered to be a cornerstone of the expected success of the project.
The formation of independent joint stock companies has been a condition of inclusion under the
project. The benefits associated with corporatization/privatization are largely a function of the
attitudes and focus of the management and employees of the GDC. The employees have become
shareholders in a joint stock company whose benefits are directly tied to the financial success of the
GDC. There is considerable evidence that attention to profitability has already been established. The
GDC has been careful with the size of the project, having reduced the size of the loan to make sure
that only its priority needs are addressed.
11.    During project implementation, institutional reform will be reinforced through
twinning/training relationships. Volgograd Gorgas would be linked to a foreign GDC who would
provide training in key areas like: (a) technical aspects such as cathodic protection, metering and
SCADA systems; (b) financial and accounting practices based on GAAP standards; (c) management
information systems; (d) marketing; and, (e) human resource development. Shareholders and the
Board of Directors are expected to take an increasingly active role in the functioning of the company.
This is expected to be further reinforced when the Government further divests of its shares. This
process would be dealt with under a study in which the Government would seek assistance from
consultants on divestiture of its remaining shares.
12.    A component of the bid evaluation process is expected to include an assessment of the
financial viability of each project component given the bid prices. Should the lowest evaluated
technically responsive bid be much higher than the estimated cost, a review of the procurement
process would take place to identify problems. If problems warrant it, they could be dealt with
through retendering. If the cost of equipment would, through some unforeseen problem, jeopardize
the project or the owner's financial viability, the component would be dropped from the project.
Analysis
13.    Each component of the project's financial costs and benefits were assigned a probability
distribution based on the uncertainties for costs and quantities of capital expenditures for each year of
implementation. For ease of representation, each expenditure was assigned a triangular distribution,
representing most likely, minimum and most likely. The stochastic nature of the process was
simulated using a conunercially available risk analysis program. The simulation made use of Latin
2   Unlike power generation and similar projects whose benefits are contingent upon completing all of the
proposed investments, this project can achieve benefits with only part of the project implemented. For example,
installing half of the meters will result in half the benefits, cathodic protection on half the pipe network will
achieve half of the life extension benefits, etc.



-139-
Annex 8-2
Page 4 of 5
Hypercube sampling to increase the accuracy of representation of the input distributions.
Results
14.   The two different subcomponents of the gas distribution component - metering and asset life
extension - were analyzed separately. The Base Case on Asset Life Extension (cathodic protection,
SCADA and pipe replacement) resulted in an expected FIRR of 23.7 percent. This is less than that
indicated by the deterministic evaluation of the FIRR because the probability distribution of the
benefits are negatively skewed. The analysis was rerun, assuming that, as a result of pro-active
support of Russian manufacturers by the GDCs, Russian manufacturers would compete for the supply
contracts (Scenario 1). This was modelled by increasing the probability of a low priced bid. As a
result of attracting interest of Russian manufacturers, the expected FIRR increased to 25%. The
second scenario added the impact of modifying the bidding process if the bid prices were higher than
expected. This scenario (Scenario 2) assumed that if bid prices were higher than an acceptable
margin, bidding documents would be revised and rebidding would take place. The results of this
action would increase the expected FIRR to 25.8%. Scenario 4 added the impact of increasing
project implementation efficiency. This is expected to take place as a result of the formation of joint
stock companies, combined with the comprehensive twinning/training. This resulted in further
increasing the expected FIRR to 26.5%. Figure 1 summarizes the results of the incremental impact
of these assumptions.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION

PRO FORMA FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
1.    This Annex details the assumptions and findings of the analysis of the financial performance of
Volgograd Gorgas, incorporating the financial commitments associated with the project, and summarizes
the findings with regard to the project's financial viability in terms of the cash flow and debt servicing
capabilities of the borrower, and of the projected ability to comply with financial covenants.
A.    Cash Flow and Self-financing Analysis
2.    A review was carried out to examine the ability of Volgograd Gorgas to finance the local share
of the project costs and also to adequately meet reasonable debt service and return-on-asset requirements
over the project life. Cash-flow projections were developed based on current and projected sales, sales
margins, and operating costs, and incorporating the cash flows associated with financing the projects and
meeting debt service requirements.
3.    Base financial figures were obtained for 1992 and 1993. Projections of revenues were based on
forecasts of sales volumes and retail prices. Operating costs were projected based on wholesale prices
associated with the retail price forecasts, and on inflationary adjustments to 1993 cost levels. VAT has
been excluded from both the purchase and selling prices of gas, since it is essentially a pass-through item
from the perspective of the utilities. Balance sheet accounts were generally adjusted with inflation,
through links to sales and expenditure projections (for example, inventories, accounts payable and cash-in-
bank were linked to operating costs, accounts receivable were linked to projected sales).
4.    Income statements have been prepared based on a GAAP style accrual basis. This could overstate
the corporate income tax liability, particularly during the first one to two years while the accruals
problem is being resolved. Over the longer term, however, the accrual basis is likely to conform to
actual statements as utilities follow the general move to accrual-style accounting.
5.    The key assumptions in the cash-flow analysis are summarized on Page 2. Page 3 provides
highlights of the findings. Pro forma income statements, balance sheets and source and application of
funds statements are included on Pages 4 through 6.
